Voice Analysis: Alan Hirsch

Voice Summary

Alan Hirsch writes and speaks with a distinctive blend of prophetic urgency, theological depth, and practical accessibility. His voice is that of a movement catalyst who operates at the intersection of academic rigor and grassroots activism -- he builds systematic frameworks (mDNA, APEST) but always in service of igniting action, not merely informing minds. His tone carries a persistent sense of spiritual urgency: the Western church is in crisis, the answer lies in recovering ancient patterns, and the time to act is now.

Writing Characteristics

Dimension Assessment
Register Accessible complexity -- integrates academic concepts (liminality, communitas, missiology) with clear explanations accessible to educated lay leaders. Not purely academic; not purely pastoral.
Complexity Moderate to Complex -- builds multi-layered frameworks with systematic rigor, but uses diagrams, lists, and practical examples to keep material graspable. Varies by audience: 5Q and The Permanent Revolution are more complex; Right Here, Right Now is more accessible.
Tone Prophetic-Apostolic -- combines the urgency of a prophet ("the church must change or die") with the constructive vision of an apostle ("here is the framework for how"). Occasionally provocative, always purposeful. Warm toward fellow travelers; sharp toward complacency and institutionalism.
Storytelling Heavy -- draws extensively on personal narrative (South Africa, conversion, South Melbourne), historical movements (early church, Chinese house churches, Celtic missions), and contemporary case studies. Stories serve as evidence for frameworks, not mere illustration.
Humor Occasional -- dry, self-deprecating Australian humor surfaces in interviews and spoken contexts. Less present in written work. Will use humor to disarm before making a provocative point.
Scripture Use Moderate -- uses Scripture strategically rather than exhaustively. Key passages are foundational (Ephesians 4:11 for APEST, the Shema, Christological passages). Does not proof-text; integrates biblical theology with missiological argument. Emphasizes Hebraic reading over Western systematic theology.

Signature Patterns

  • Recurring phrases:

    • "missional-incarnational" (hyphenated compound -- his signature descriptor)
    • "movemental" (coined term -- church as movement, not institution)
    • "Apostolic Genius" (the latent movement DNA in every church)
    • "mDNA" (missional DNA -- the six elements)
    • "the forgotten ways" (what the church has lost and must recover)
    • "liminality and communitas" (growth through shared ordeal)
    • "deep change" (not surface reform but paradigm transformation)
    • "Jesus is Lord" (the organizing center that gates everything)
    • "not X but Y" contrast structures (see below)

  • Favorite metaphors:

    • DNA/genetics: The church has missional DNA that lies dormant and must be "reactivated." APEST functions as "theo-genetic code."
    • Virus/organic spread: Movements spread "like viruses" -- decentralized, organic, unstoppable by institutions.
    • Seed/womb: "The seed of the future is in the womb of the present."
    • Starfish vs. spider: Decentralized (starfish) organizations vs. centralized (spider) ones -- borrowed from Brafman/Beckstrom but deeply integrated.
    • Recovery/reactivation: The language of recovering something lost rather than inventing something new. The ways are "forgotten," not absent.
    • Exile/return: The APE ministries (Apostle, Prophet, Evangelist) have been "exiled" from the church and must return.

  • Argument structure: Hirsch typically builds a case through:

    1. Provocation -- a sharp observation about the failure or crisis of the current paradigm
    2. Historical/biblical grounding -- evidence from the early church, Chinese house churches, or Scripture showing the alternative
    3. Framework introduction -- a named model or diagram that systematizes the insight
    4. Practical implication -- what this means for leaders, churches, and movements today
    5. Call to action -- urgent invitation to embrace the paradigm shift

  • Opening style: Often begins with a provocative question or a sharp diagnosis of what is wrong. Rarely eases in gently -- leads with the problem or the vision, then unpacks.

  • Contrast structures: A hallmark of Hirsch's rhetoric is the "not X but Y" or "from X to Y" pairing:

    • "Not programs but paradigm; not maintenance but movement"
    • "Not attractional but incarnational"
    • "Not institution but movement"
    • "From Christendom to missional"
    • "From shepherd-teacher to full APEST" These binary contrasts serve as cognitive disruption -- forcing the reader to choose a paradigm.

Sample Passages

"Phenomenal Jesus movements grow precisely because they do not have centralized institutions to block growth through control. Remarkable Jesus movements have the feel of a movement, have structure as a network, and spread like viruses." -- The Forgotten Ways

"The book you are holding is a plea to the church to reawaken the dormant potential within it, to rediscover the power that once turned the world upside down." -- The Forgotten Ways, characterizing the book's purpose

"Christology determines missiology, and missiology determines ecclesiology. Get this sequence wrong, and everything else gets distorted." -- ReJesus (paraphrased; the core argument of the book)

"For me, coming to Jesus was coming home in every way. I had an overwhelming encounter with God and was totally and absolutely won over. It was the defining moment of my life: I gave my life fully to Jesus and I have never looked back." -- Untamed, Introduction

Speaking vs. Writing

Hirsch's spoken voice is noticeably warmer, more informal, and more humorous than his written work. In podcast interviews and conference talks:

  • More personal: He tells more stories from his own life, South African upbringing, and ministry failures. The Australian informality comes through -- he calls himself "Al," uses self-deprecating humor, and is comfortable with tangents.
  • More passionate: The prophetic urgency in his writing becomes visceral passion in speaking. His voice rises when talking about the crisis of the Western church or the potential of movements.
  • More conversational: He engages with interviewers genuinely, asks rhetorical questions, and builds on dialogue rather than lecturing.
  • Same intellectual framework: Whether speaking or writing, the same frameworks (mDNA, APEST) and the same contrast structures ("not X but Y") are present. The conceptual architecture is consistent across modes.
  • Australian accent and idiom: His spoken voice carries an Australian accent and occasional Australian/South African expressions that add warmth and distinctiveness. This personal texture is naturally absent from written work.

The overall effect is that Hirsch's written work reads as the systematic, carefully constructed version of ideas that he communicates with more fire and personality when speaking. Both modes serve the same mission: catalyzing movement.

Source: profile/theology.md (source: docs/movement_leader_research/alan-hirsch/profile/theology.md)

Theological Profile: Alan Hirsch

Core Thesis

The Western church has lost the essential DNA of apostolic movements -- what Hirsch calls "Apostolic Genius" -- and must recover these forgotten ways to become again the dynamic, multiplying, Jesus-centered movement it was designed to be. The church is not fundamentally an institution but a movement, and recovering its missional DNA requires a paradigm shift from Christendom-mode maintenance to apostolic-mode multiplication.

Theological Tradition

Primary: Missional-Incarnational, with roots in the Stone-Campbell Restoration Movement (Churches of Christ in Australia)

Secondary influences: Anabaptist-influenced ecclesiology, Jewish-Hebraic thought (from personal heritage), post-Christendom missiology (Newbigin school), charismatic/Pentecostal movement dynamics (studied rather than claimed as personal tradition)

Denominational context: Ordained/credentialed within the Churches of Christ in Australia (Victoria and Tasmania Conference). Not bound to a single tradition; works across denominational lines.

Key Frameworks & Models

Framework Description Where Developed
mDNA (Missional DNA) Six interdependent elements of "Apostolic Genius" that characterize every significant Jesus movement: (1) Jesus is Lord, (2) Disciple Making, (3) Missional-Incarnational Impulse, (4) Apostolic Environment (APEST), (5) Organic Systems, (6) Communitas (Liminality-Communitas). "Jesus is Lord" gates overall maturity. The Forgotten Ways (2006), The Forgotten Ways Handbook (2009)
APEST / 5Q Five-fold ministry gifts from Ephesians 4:11 -- Apostles, Prophets, Evangelists, Shepherds, Teachers -- understood not just as leadership roles but as the "original intelligence and capacity" of the Body of Christ. All five are needed for a fully functioning church. The Permanent Revolution (2012), 5Q (2017), Activating 5Q (2018)
Incarnational Mission Mission follows the pattern of the Incarnation: God enters culture rather than extracting people from it. Missional engagement means "living among" not "reaching out to." Contextual, relational, embedded, everyday. The Shaping of Things to Come (2003)
Metanoia Repentance understood as fundamental paradigm-level transformation that changes everything -- individuals, churches, organizations -- from the inside out. Not merely moral reform but cognitive-spiritual revolution. Metanoia (2019)
Movemental Thinking The distinction between institution and movement as organizing paradigms for the church. Movements are organic, multiplicative, network-structured, virus-like in spread. Institutions are centralized, controlling, maintenance-focused. Throughout all works; central to The Forgotten Ways, On the Verge
Christology > Missiology > Ecclesiology A cascading theological sequence: who Jesus is determines how we do mission, which determines what the church looks like. Get Christology wrong and everything downstream is distorted. ReJesus (2008/2009)
Theo-genetic codes The concept that ministry types (APEST) function like genetic codes within the Body of Christ -- each containing distinct "intelligence" that the church needs to be healthy and complete. The Permanent Revolution (2012)
Reframation The process of changing mental frameworks to transform understanding of God, people, and mission. Re-enchanting worldview through fresh theological lenses. Reframation (2018)

Distinctive Positions

  • APEST is for all Christians, not just leaders: The five-fold gifts are not merely leadership offices but callings distributed across the entire Body of Christ. Every believer has an APEST profile.
  • The Western church has essentially exiled three of five ministries: Apostolic, Prophetic, and Evangelistic functions have been marginalized in favor of Shepherd and Teacher, creating an imbalanced, maintenance-oriented church.
  • "Jesus is Lord" gates everything: In the mDNA framework, no element can rise above the church's Christological maturity. If "Jesus is Lord" is at Level 2, the whole system is capped at Level 2.
  • Movement over institution is normative: The New Testament church was a movement, not an institution. Institutionalization is deviation from the apostolic norm, not maturation toward it.
  • Every believer is a missionary: Mission is not a department of the church or a role for specialists; it is the fundamental identity of every follower of Jesus in their sphere of influence.
  • Hebraic vs. Hellenistic thought: Hirsch consistently distinguishes between Hebraic (holistic, embodied, dynamic) and Hellenistic (dualistic, abstract, static) modes of thinking, arguing the Western church has been too shaped by the latter.
  • Organic systems over organizational complexity: Simple, reproducible patterns that can multiply are preferred over complex, centralized structures that require professional management.
  • Communitas through liminality: Authentic community (communitas) is forged through shared ordeal and risk, not through comfort and programs. The church needs adventure and liminality to experience true fellowship.

Intellectual Genealogy

Shaped by:

  • Lesslie Newbigin -- Missional theology, the church as sign/instrument/foretaste of the kingdom, post-Christendom missiology
  • Roland Allen -- Organic, Spirit-led mission; indigenous church principles; critique of Western missionary methods
  • Dietrich Bonhoeffer -- Costly discipleship, religionless Christianity, the church's role in the world
  • Victor Turner -- Anthropological concepts of liminality and communitas (adapted for theological use)
  • Howard Snyder -- Radical renewal movements in church history
  • Chinese house church movement / early church movements -- Studied as living examples of Apostolic Genius in action
  • Jewish-Hebraic tradition -- Personal heritage; Shema spirituality; embodied faith; Jesus as Jewish Messiah

In conversation with:

  • Michael Frost (closest collaborator; incarnational mission)
  • Tim Catchim (apostolic ministry and APEST theory)
  • Dave Ferguson (megachurch transformation; multiplication)
  • Ed Stetzer (missiological research; church planting)
  • Lance Ford (practical missional living; decentralized leadership)
  • Neil Cole (organic church; simple church movements)
  • Reggie McNeal (missional leadership; church culture change)
  • Todd Hunter (Anglican missional bishop; C4SO network)

Pushes back against:

  • Christendom model: The assumption that the church occupies a central, privileged position in culture. Hirsch argues this era is over and the church must learn to be missional rather than attractional.
  • Attractional church paradigm: The "come and see" model where the church builds programs to attract people. Hirsch advocates "go and be" incarnational presence instead.
  • Shepherd-Teacher dominance: The reduction of church leadership to only pastoral care and teaching, which Hirsch argues truncates the Body of Christ and eliminates apostolic, prophetic, and evangelistic energy.
  • Institutional preservation: The tendency to make the institution's survival the primary goal, rather than multiplication of mission.
  • Hellenistic dualism: The separation of sacred/secular, spiritual/physical that Hirsch sees as a distortion imported from Greek philosophy rather than reflecting biblical (Hebraic) holism.

Theological Evolution

Hirsch's theology has been remarkably consistent in its core convictions since his first major publication in 2003, but has deepened and expanded in specific areas:

  1. Early phase (2003-2006): Foundational missional-incarnational theology developed with Michael Frost in The Shaping of Things to Come. The Forgotten Ways established the mDNA framework as the core intellectual contribution.

  2. APEST deepening (2012-2018): From general mention of "apostolic environment" as one mDNA element, Hirsch developed APEST into a comprehensive framework with its own assessment tools, training programs, and organizational applications. The shift from The Forgotten Ways to 5Q represents a significant deepening of what was originally one element among six.

  3. Transformation/inner change emphasis (2018-2020): Metanoia and Reframation signal a turn toward the interior dimensions of transformation -- how paradigm shifts actually happen at the cognitive and spiritual level. This complements the earlier structural/organizational focus.

  4. Movement scaling (2016-present): The founding of 100Movements represents a shift from theoretical writing to active organizational catalysis of movements globally.

Throughout, "Jesus is Lord" has remained the non-negotiable center, and the fundamental conviction that the church is a movement (not an institution) has never wavered.

Source: hirsch-affinity-rubric.md (source: docs/movement_leader_research/alan-hirsch/hirsch-affinity-rubric.md)

Hirsch Affinity Rubric (0–100)

Purpose

This rubric measures the conceptual and relational affinity of any published author to Alan Hirsch—i.e., how closely their thought, practice, and positioning align with Hirsch’s body of work. It is designed to be completed without reading Hirsch’s original books, using organic search and agentic knowledge: patterns discoverable through web search, citations, affiliations, and observable language.

Use cases:

  • Ranking authors by Hirsch affinity for content discovery
  • Search-and-classification pipelines for movement-leader research
  • Mapping author clusters in missional/APEST/movement space
  • Identifying hidden affinities (authors who think like Hirsch but don’t cite him)

Design Principle: Search-Completable Without Book Access

Each dimension is scored from evidence that can be found via:

  • Web search (articles, bios, interviews)
  • Citation / reference analysis
  • Organizational affiliations and conference rosters
  • Co-authorship and endorsement networks
  • Observable vocabulary and conceptual patterns
  • AI / agent summaries of publicly available content

Not required: Direct access to The Forgotten Ways, Shaping of Things to Come, 5Q, or other Hirsch books.

Hirsch Signature Concepts (Reference for Assessors)

Use these as the conceptual anchor. Affinity is higher when an author references, builds on, or independently expresses these ideas.

Concept Observable Signals
APEST / 5Q Fivefold ministry (Apostle, Prophet, Evangelist, Shepherd, Teacher); apostolic environments; Ephesians 4 leadership
mDNA / Apostolic Genius Missional DNA; six elements (Jesus is Lord, disciple-making, apostolic environment, missional-incarnational impulse, organic systems, communitas); “reactivating latent” language
Centered set vs. bounded set Community defined by movement toward Jesus rather than boundaries; drawing people closer vs. defining in/out
Incarnational mission 6 P’s (Presence, Proximity, Prevenience, Passion, Powerlessness, Proclamation); “you can’t commute to mission”; embodied witness
Organic movement vs. institution Small, reproducible, networked structures; multiplication over addition; movement DNA over church-growth formulas
Communitas Community forged in liminality, shared mission, risk; beyond comfort-based community
Disciple-making systems Obedience-based, reproducible, relational discipleship; not information transfer
Missional church Church as sent, not gathered-to-consume; mission-shaped ecclesiology

0–100 Rubric: Hirsch Affinity

Category A: Direct Hirsch Engagement (0–25 points)

Evidence: Explicit citation, collaboration, or adoption of Hirsch frameworks

Score Criteria Search Hints

18–21 Repeatedly cites Hirsch; teaches his frameworks; uses APEST/5Q as primary lens “Alan Hirsch,” “Forgotten Ways,” “5Q,” “APEST” in their content
13–17 Clear references to Hirsch; endorses or recommends his work Book endorsements, interview mentions, recommended reading
7–12 Occasional mention; uses some Hirsch-derived language Single citations, conference co-appearance
1–6 Minimal or no direct reference Rare/none in searchable content
0 No detectable engagement —

Evidence: Organizational, conference, and collaboration overlap

Score Criteria Search Hints

5–8 Occasional overlap; same publisher (IVP) or similar audience Publisher, audience overlap
1–4 Distant or no network overlap —
0 No shared networks —

Evidence: Use of Hirsch-signature ideas and language, even without naming him

Score Criteria Search Hints
26–30 Uses APEST/5Q, mDNA, centered set, incarnational 6 P’s, communitas; movement-over-institution framing “APEST,” “centered set,” “incarnational,” “communitas,” “missional DNA,” “organic movement”
20–25 Strong overlap: incarnational mission, organic systems, disciple-making, sent church “Incarnational,” “multiplication,” “disciple-making,” “sent,” “organic”
14–19 Moderate overlap: missional church, kingdom mission, multiplication language “Missional,” “kingdom,” “multiplication,” “movement”
8–13 Partial overlap: some movement/sending language; reform-oriented “Church planting,” “mission,” “discipleship”
2–7 Weak overlap: occasional missional or formation language Sparse matches
0–1 No discernible conceptual overlap —

Category D: Theological Posture (0–15 points)

Evidence: Posture toward church, mission, and culture that aligns with Hirsch

Score Criteria Search Hints
13–15 Movement over institution; reforming not defending; empowering not controlling; presence over programs “Movement,” “reform,” “empower,” “presence,” “practices” vs. “programs”
10–12 Clear movement/incarnational posture; challenges attractional model Critique of “attractional,” “institutional”
6–9 Some reform language; kingdom-oriented “Kingdom,” “renewal,” “transformation”
2–5 Mixed or ambiguous —
0–1 Institution-preserving; attractional; no reform posture —

Category E: Independent Conceptual Resonance (0–10 points)

Evidence: Ideas that mirror Hirsch without clear derivation—suggests shared DNA

Score Criteria Search Hints
9–10 Develops frameworks that closely parallel mDNA, APEST, or incarnational 6 P’s without citing Hirsch Comparable frameworks (e.g., BELLS-like, multiplication models)
6–8 Clear parallel concepts (e.g., centered set, liminal community) “Liminal,” “centered,” “organic,” “multiplication” in distinctive ways
3–5 Some resonant themes —
0–2 No notable resonance —

Search Process for Ranked Affinity List

Phase 1: Candidate Discovery

Run these searches to surface candidates; capture name, primary domain, and 1–2 source URLs.

"Alan Hirsch" co-author collaborator
"Forge" network faculty leaders missional
"100Movements" "5Q Collective" leaders
"APEST" OR "5Q" OR "fivefold ministry" author
"missional church" author 2020-
"Forgotten Ways" cited recommended
"centered set" "bounded set" church author
"incarnational mission" author practitioner
"Shaping of Things to Come" cited Frost Hirsch
"missional DNA" OR "mDNA" author
"communitas" missional author

Phase 2: Per-Candidate Evidence Gathering

For each candidate:

  1. Direct engagement: Search "[candidate name]" "Alan Hirsch" and "[candidate name]" "Forgotten Ways" OR "5Q" OR "APEST"
  2. Network: Search "[candidate name]" Forge OR 100Movements OR 5Q OR Exponential OR Missio Alliance
  3. Concepts: Search "[candidate name]" incarnational OR missional OR multiplication OR centered set
  4. Content sample: Pull 2–3 representative articles, bios, or book descriptions for Category C–E

Phase 3: Scoring

  1. Score each category using the rubric tables.
  2. Sum for total (0–100).
  3. Record evidence source (URL or snippet) for each score.

Phase 4: Ranking and Classification

Score Band Label Interpretation
85–100 Core Inner circle; co-authors, org leaders, primary articulators of Hirsch’s frameworks
65–84 High Affinity Strong conceptual and/or network alignment; likely cite and teach Hirsch
45–64 Moderate Affinity Meaningful overlap; missional/movement language; some network proximity
25–44 Low Affinity Partial overlap; tangential to Hirsch’s space
0–24 Minimal / No Affinity Little discernible connection

Output Format

Ranked List (CSV/Markdown)

Rank Name Score A B C D E Domain Evidence Notes
1 Michael Frost 92 21 18 28 15 10 Incarnational mission Co-author Shaping; Forge; BELLS
2 Brad Brisco 78 15 14 24 15 10 Missional church Forge; repeated Hirsch refs
… … … … … … … … … …

Classification Tags (Optional)

  • core / high / moderate / low / minimal
  • cited_hirsch / network_overlap / conceptual_only / independent_resonance

Benchmarks (Illustrative)

Author A B C D E Total Notes

Agent Instructions

When using this rubric via an AI agent:

  1. Prefer observable evidence—cite URLs, snippets, or search results for each score.
  2. Flag uncertainty—if evidence is thin, note “low confidence” and the missing evidence type.
  3. Do not assume—no points for concepts not found in searchable content.
  4. Cross-check—if a candidate scores high on C–E but 0 on A–B, consider “hidden affinity” and validate with extra searches.
  5. Update benchmarks—as new candidates are scored, add representative examples to the benchmark table.

This rubric is designed for search-based, agentic assessment of Hirsch affinity without requiring access to Hirsch’s books. It complements the general Movemental thought leader rubric in tam-search/03-RUBRIC.md, which measures platform readiness and movemental DNA across domains.

  • Agent instructions followed: Observable evidence preferred; uncertainty flagged where evidence thin; no points for concepts not found in searchable content.

Publisher Page https://bakerpublishinggroup.com/books/the-forgotten-ways-2nd-edition/2679716

Description: Hirsch's paradigm-shifting classic -- the definitive statement of the church as dynamic missional movement. Presents a vision for the future based on a first-century paradigm, asserting that traditional church growth formulas no longer work. Explores the concept of Apostolic Genius (mDNA) as the latent potential embedded in every Christian community. The 2nd edition includes fresh examples, expanded glossary, new appendices, and an index.

Key Themes: mDNA framework, apostolic movement, missional church, Apostolic Genius, church movements

Awards: Widely regarded as one of the most influential books on missional church in the 21st century.

Editorial Roles

Alan Hirsch serves as:

  • Series Editor: Baker Books' Shapevine Missional Series
  • Series Editor: IVP's Forge line
  • Associate Editor: Leadership Journal

These roles involve contributing editorial vision, introductions, and curation rather than bylined articles.

Key Observations

  1. Volume: Hirsch's written article output is moderate relative to his book output. Most of his public-facing content comes through books, speaking, and organizational platforms rather than regular article writing.
  2. Distribution: Content is spread across many platforms rather than concentrated in one outlet. No regular column or contributor relationship with a major publication is evident.
  3. Recency: Most discoverable articles are from 2016-2021. Blog activity on alanhirsch.org is irregular.
  4. Format shift: More recent content appears to be delivered through organizational channels (100Movements, 5Q Central, Ephesiology) and course platforms rather than traditional article publishing.
  5. Gap opportunity: Regular, consistent article/blog publishing represents a significant content opportunity.

Academic Publications: Alan Hirsch

Google Scholar

Field Value
Profile URL Not found (no dedicated Google Scholar profile identified)
h-index N/A
Total citations N/A
i10-index N/A

Note: Alan Hirsch does not appear to maintain a Google Scholar, Academia.edu, or ResearchGate profile. His academic output is primarily practitioner-oriented (books, training programs, frameworks) rather than peer-reviewed journal publications. This is consistent with his role as a movement leader, strategist, and organizational founder rather than a traditional academic researcher.

Publications

Alan Hirsch's published works are overwhelmingly practitioner-focused books (see books.md for full bibliography). However, his work is widely cited in academic literature on missional ecclesiology, church planting, and leadership studies.

Key Observations

  1. Practitioner-theorist: Hirsch's academic influence is primarily through his books and frameworks rather than peer-reviewed journal articles. He is cited extensively in academic work by others.
  2. Framework adoption: The APEST/5Q and mDNA frameworks have been adopted and studied in academic contexts, particularly in missiology, ecclesiology, and practical theology.
  3. Assessment data: The 5Q/APEST assessment (150,000+ completions) represents a significant dataset that could be leveraged for academic research.
  4. No formal Google Scholar presence: Unlike traditional academics, Hirsch does not maintain profiles on academic platforms (Google Scholar, Academia.edu, ResearchGate).
  5. Australian University of Theology: ReJesus is listed in the publications of the Australian University of Theology, suggesting some formal academic recognition in Australia.

Identity Verification Notes

  • Confirmed identity: Alan Hirsch, the Australian missional church leader, founder of Forge Missional Training Network, 100Movements, and 5Q Collective. NOT the South African economist, NOT the American law professor/false confessions expert.
  • Some search results returned the law professor Alan Hirsch (false confessions) and the South African economist Alan Hirsch (SOEs) — these were excluded from this catalog.

Accessibility Notes

  • Most podcast guest episodes are freely available on Apple Podcasts, Spotify, and podcast host websites.
  • Exponential conference recordings are typically behind a paid Digital Access Pass.
  • Audiobooks require purchase through Audible or subscription through Everand/OverDrive.
  • The 5QCast is freely available on Apple Podcasts.

Key Observations

  1. No personal YouTube channel: Unlike many contemporary Christian leaders, Alan Hirsch does not maintain a personal YouTube channel. His video presence is entirely through third-party platforms and conference recordings.

  2. Verge Conference dominance: The Verge Network/Conference is the single largest source of his video content, with 10+ clips from conference appearances (~2010-2013 era).

  3. Exponential as recurring platform: Hirsch has spoken at Exponential conferences repeatedly (2014, 2016, 2017, 2019, 2020, 2022), making it his most consistent conference relationship.

  4. Podcast era shift: Post-2020, his video appearances have shifted heavily toward podcast video formats (Fresh Expressions, Church Digital, Everyday Disciple, Ephesiology, Canadian Church Leaders, Microchurches).

  5. alanhirsch.org/videos: His personal website aggregates video content but does not appear to host original video content directly.

  6. 5Q Central / theforgottenways.org: These owned platforms contain video resources but much content is behind registration or purchase.

  7. Signature themes across all videos: Missional church, APEST/5Q fivefold ministry, discipleship vs. consumerism, communitas, apostolic movements, Ephesians 4, Reframation/Metanoia.

  8. International reach: Content appears on Australian (Exponential Australia, Morling College), UK (Anthony Delaney/LAUNCH), Canadian (Canadian Church Leaders), and US platforms.

  9. Morling College video series: The 6-session "Missiology To Go: Fivefold Leadership" course with the Tinsley Institute represents his most structured video teaching content.

  10. Awakenings 2023 plenary: The "Metanoia" talk is notable as a major recent conference appearance, with video available for purchase through Missio Alliance.

Key Observations

  1. Multi-platform: Training is distributed across Ephesiology, 5Q Central, Forge (Teachable), Future Travelers, and academic institutions.
  2. Framework-centered: All courses center on Hirsch's core frameworks (APEST/5Q, mDNA, missional movement).
  3. Assessment integration: The 5Q/APEST assessment (150,000+ taken) is deeply integrated into the training ecosystem.
  4. Gap: No widely accessible, low-cost or free standalone online course on a major platform (Udemy, Coursera, etc.). Training is primarily through proprietary channels.
  5. Academic credibility: Adjunct faculty positions at multiple seminaries lend academic weight to the training content.

Source: ALAN_HIRSCH_CONTENT_AUDIT.md (source: docs/movement_leader_research/alan-hirsch/ALAN_HIRSCH_CONTENT_AUDIT.md)

Alan Hirsch: Content Audit

Comprehensive content inventory with performance analysis, gap identification, and repurposing opportunities

Version: 1.0.0
Word Count: 1,300
Expert Perspective: Content marketing, content strategy, content repurposing, SEO optimization

Executive Summary

This audit provides a comprehensive inventory of Alan Hirsch's existing content assets across all formats and distribution channels. It catalogs 17+ published books, identifies content strengths and gaps, maps distribution channels, analyzes performance opportunities, and outlines repurposing strategies. The audit reveals a strong foundational content library with coherent frameworks (mDNA, APEST) but identifies opportunities for increased digital content frequency, multimedia formats, and updated foundational content for current contexts.

Key Outcomes:

  • Complete content inventory by format and topic
  • Content performance analysis and opportunities
  • Content gap identification
  • Repurposing roadmap
  • Content architecture recommendations

Content Inventory Matrix

Books (17+ Published Works)

Content Performance Analysis

High-Performing Content

1. Foundational Books (The Forgotten Ways, The Shaping of Things to Come)

  • Performance: Widely referenced, foundational texts
  • Why: Core frameworks, comprehensive coverage
  • Strategy: Maintain as foundational, repurpose extensively

2. Framework Books (5Q, The Permanent Revolution)

  • Performance: High engagement, practical application
  • Why: Clear frameworks, actionable content
  • Strategy: Repurpose into courses, assessments, articles

3. Practical Guides (The Forgotten Ways Handbook, Activating 5Q)

  • Performance: High utility, implementation-focused
  • Why: Practical, actionable, tool-oriented
  • Strategy: Repurpose into courses, tools, articles

Medium-Performing Content

4. Practical Application Books (Right Here, Right Now, Untamed)

  • Performance: Good engagement, accessible
  • Why: Practical, everyday application
  • Strategy: Repurpose into articles, videos, courses

5. Collaborative Works (ReJesus, The Starfish and the Spirit)

  • Performance: Good engagement, specialized topics
  • Why: Thematic focus, collaborative voice
  • Strategy: Repurpose into articles, courses

Low-Performing Content

6. Specialized Works (Disciplism, The Faith of Leap)

  • Performance: Lower engagement, specialized topics
  • Why: Narrow focus, less known
  • Strategy: Repurpose into articles, integrate into broader content

Content Gap Analysis

High-Priority Gaps

1. Recent Digital Content

  • Gap: Limited recent articles, videos, podcasts
  • Impact: Audience wants current, accessible content
  • Priority: High
  • Opportunity: Regular blog posts, video teaching, podcast presence

2. Multimedia Formats

  • Gap: Limited video, podcast, interactive content
  • Impact: Missing engagement opportunities
  • Priority: High
  • Opportunity: Video series, regular podcast, interactive tools

3. Updated Foundational Content

  • Gap: Some foundational works are older (2003-2011)
  • Impact: Content may feel dated
  • Priority: High
  • Opportunity: Updated editions, current context applications

4. Case Studies & Examples

  • Gap: Limited real-world examples, case studies
  • Impact: Leaders want to see frameworks applied
  • Priority: High
  • Opportunity: Case study content, success stories, practical examples

5. Content for Younger Leaders

  • Gap: Limited content specifically for digital natives, younger leaders
  • Impact: Missing next generation
  • Priority: Medium
  • Opportunity: Content tailored for younger audiences, digital-first formats

Medium-Priority Gaps

6. Interactive Tools & Assessments

  • Gap: Need more accessible, interactive assessment tools
  • Impact: Leaders want diagnostic tools
  • Priority: Medium
  • Opportunity: Enhanced mDNA assessment, APEST tools, interactive resources

7. Digital/Online Missional Practice

  • Gap: How movement works in networked, virtual spaces
  • Impact: Need frameworks for digital mission
  • Priority: Medium
  • Opportunity: Content on digital missional practice, online community

8. Non-Western Global Expressions

  • Gap: Limited engagement with non-Western church expressions
  • Impact: Missing global perspectives
  • Priority: Medium
  • Opportunity: Content co-created with non-Western leaders, global case studies

Repurposing Roadmap

High-Value Repurposing Opportunities

1. Books → Courses

  • Source: The Forgotten Ways, 5Q, The Permanent Revolution
  • Target: Comprehensive courses on core frameworks
  • Format: Week-based or module-based courses
  • Priority: Very High
  • Estimated Value: High (revenue, engagement, reach)

2. Books → Articles

  • Source: All books (chapter-by-chapter)
  • Target: Long-form articles, blog posts
  • Format: SEO-optimized articles
  • Priority: High
  • Estimated Value: High (SEO, reach, engagement)

3. Speaking → Videos

  • Source: Conference recordings, teaching sessions
  • Target: Video series, teaching content
  • Format: YouTube, owned platform
  • Priority: High
  • Estimated Value: High (engagement, reach)

4. Books → Assessments

  • Source: 5Q, The Forgotten Ways, The Permanent Revolution
  • Target: Interactive assessments, diagnostic tools
  • Format: Online assessments
  • Priority: High
  • Estimated Value: Very High (conversion, engagement)

5. Practical Guides → Tools

  • Source: The Forgotten Ways Handbook, Activating 5Q
  • Target: Interactive tools, worksheets, resources
  • Format: Downloadable tools, interactive resources
  • Priority: Medium
  • Estimated Value: Medium (utility, engagement)

Discoverability (SEO) 4 Wikipedia page, multiple organizational profiles, publisher pages, and Amazon presence provide strong SEO signals; ranks well for "Alan Hirsch missional" queries
Overall 3.2/5 Strong organizational and institutional web presence, but personal digital engagement platforms (social, email, video) are underdeveloped relative to his influence and thought leadership stature

Score Interpretation

Alan Hirsch has a broad but shallow personal digital presence. His influence is felt most strongly through:

  • His organizational network (100Movements, 5Q, Forge, MLC) which carries his frameworks
  • His published books which are well-distributed across retail and library platforms
  • His assessment tools (APEST) which drive ongoing engagement

Key gaps for Movemental to address:

  1. No centralized YouTube/video presence (significant content scattered across third-party channels)
  2. Modest email list (1.5K on Substack) relative to social following (37.9K Twitter)
  3. No Instagram or short-form video strategy
  4. Website lacks modern engagement conversion (newsletter popups, embedded content, community features)
  5. No hosted podcast (only guest appearances)

Alan Hirsch: The Fragmentation Story

Generated 2026-04-15 by the fragmentation-story skill from existing research in docs/movement_leader_research/alan-hirsch/ and docs/movement_leader_research/alan-hirsch-baseline-report.md.

The Story

Alan Hirsch has published 20 books across 7 publishers over 21 years. His Goodreads author page lists 92 editions with 16,762 total ratings — which, by the industry heuristic that 1–5% of readers rate, implies somewhere between 335,000 and 1.6 million copies read. Over 150,000 people have taken his APEST assessment. He has founded or co-founded seven organizations (100 Movements, Forge, Forge America, 5Q Collective, Movement Leaders Collective, Future Travelers, Shapevine) and holds adjunct or associate faculty appointments at four accredited seminaries (Wheaton, Fuller, Asbury, George Fox). By any measure of reach, he is the gravitational center of the global missional movement.

And virtually none of that is retrievable as a system. His books live on Amazon, Goodreads, IVP, Baker/Brazos, Audible, Everand, OverDrive, Google Books, Christianbook, and seven more retailers — but the books themselves are static text; there is no AI retrieval layer, no chapter-level search, no index of the ideas inside them. The alanhirsch.org homepage is a Squarespace site with a blog, a books page, a free-ebooks page, a tests page, and an FAQ — no prominent newsletter signup, no video hub, no course library, no structured pathway connecting his 20 books to anything a reader could do next. The APEST assessment, which 150,000 people have completed, lives on theforgottenways.org and apest.5qcentral.com — and when someone finishes it, they get a score and no onward journey. That single dead end is the most telling data point in the entire system.

His audio presence is 35+ items scattered across 22+ unique podcast feeds — 5QCast is his only hosted podcast; the other 28+ appearances are guest spots on Everyday Disciple, Shifting Culture, Fresh Expressions, Ephesiology, The Church Digital, Stetzer ChurchLeaders, DTS Table, Venture 12, Godspacelight, C4SO, Voxology, LAUNCH, Post-Christian Podcast, and more. His video presence is 38+ clips distributed across 20+ third-party channels — Verge Network (10+ clips), Exponential (5+), ChurchLeaders (4+), Missio Alliance, Anthony Delaney, Fresh Expressions, Canopy International, Morling College, Asbury Seminary, and on. He has no personal YouTube channel. His social footprint is substantial on the two platforms he cares about (Twitter/X: ~37,900 followers, Facebook: ~20,800 combined across two pages), effectively absent on the two he doesn't (Instagram: one unverified handle, TikTok: nothing). His email list is 1,500 subscribers on Substack at alanhirsch1.substack.com — 4% of his Twitter follower count, against an industry baseline of 15–25% — and the newsletter posts irregularly. Audiobooks exist for 3 of his 20 books; The Forgotten Ways, 5Q, The Shaping of Things to Come, ReJesus, and The Faith of Leap — his most important titles — are print and e-book only.

So the portrait is this: a founding figure of modern missional ecclesiology whose 20 books are indexed everywhere and searchable nowhere, whose 150,000 assessment completions produced no onward pathway, whose 38+ videos have no home, whose audiobook catalogue is 15% complete, whose email list is a tenth of what it should be, and whose Spanish, Portuguese, German, and French translations exist as files in a repo rather than content on a platform. The fragmentation is not caused by absence of work — it is caused by the work being trapped in book-shaped, retailer-shaped, and organization-shaped containers that don't talk to each other.

Informational Fragmentation

Notes, Laptop, Unpublished

  • UNKNOWN — likely very substantial, not yet catalogued. Given 21 years of book writing, seminary-level teaching across four institutions, cohort design for three training networks (Forge, Forge America, Forge International), a 6-session video course (Morling), and an active speaking circuit, the private-notes layer — Scrivener files, lecture slides, cohort facilitation guides, unpublished drafts, translation working files — is almost certainly larger than the public-facing corpus.
  • Translation working files: Spanish translations exist for 10 books, Portuguese for 4+, German and French for 1 each — per the baseline report these exist as files in a repo rather than on any platform.

Embodied / In Their Head

  • The entire mDNA framework was developed embodied through years of coaching before being systematized into The Forgotten Ways.
  • The APEST / 5Q synthesis of Ephesians 4 — widely taught but the full application set travels with him as an embodied teacher.
  • Relational formation of movement leaders — decades of coaching through Forge, MLC, 100M that is not documented publicly.
  • Judgment about whether a given church can make the missional transition — Future Travelers runs 2–3 year transformation processes that rest on this embodied discernment.
  • The Australian / Global-South / U.S. bridge — he is one of the only figures who can translate between the Australian charismatic-missional tradition and the U.S. evangelical one; that translational capacity is relational, not written down.

Where Relationships Are Actually Stored

  • Mental rolodex + Debra Hirsch — almost certainly the primary store. 30+ years of founding seven organizations, adjunct faculty at four seminaries, recurring speaking at Exponential / Awakenings / Verge, and co-authorship with 11+ named collaborators means the relational graph lives primarily in his memory and his marriage.
  • 100 Movements / MLC back-ends — contain the invitation-only network data; not publicly visible.
  • Forge America cohort rosters — live with Rowland Smith's team on the Wix back-end.
  • Publisher author files — fragmented across 100M Publishing, Baker, Hendrickson, Zondervan, Jossey-Bass, Exponential Resources, Missio Publishing.
  • Seminary rosters — Wheaton, Fuller, Asbury, George Fox — FERPA-bound.
  • Substack list (1,500) — the only owned, exportable email list.
  • Twitter followers (37,900) — owned by the platform, not him.
  • No unified CRM surfaced in the research.

Synthesis

Alan Hirsch converts intellectual authority into embodied outcomes — speaking, consulting, organization-founding — at near-singular scale. But the system leaks ~90% of its potential reach before it gets to anyone who could be formed by it. A concrete picture: 150,000 APEST completions leading to 1,500 Substack subscribers (a 1% onward-journey conversion); 37,900 Twitter followers leading to 1,500 Substack subscribers (4% vs. 15–25% industry baseline); 38+ videos distributed across 20+ channels with zero personal YouTube home; 20 books of which only 3 have Audible audiobooks; translations in four languages existing as files, not content. The single largest pool of trapped value is the APEST dead-end — 150,000 people who demonstrated behavioral interest and received no structured pathway; a 10% conversion would be 15,000 new engaged users, a 30% conversion would be 45,000. The second-largest pool is his cohort-and-seminary teaching content — years of material at Wheaton, Fuller, Asbury, George Fox, and inside MLC / 100M / Forge / Future Travelers that is visible only to the people in the room.

Key Observations

  1. Founder-leader pattern: Alan Hirsch's primary organizational mode is founding and leading organizations rather than serving on external boards. His network is built around organizations he created.

  2. Ecosystem coherence: The founded organizations form a coherent ecosystem -- 100 Movements provides the vision, MLC develops leaders, 5Q provides the APEST framework, Forge provides training, Future Travelers targets large churches, and Shapevine provides the resource platform.

  3. Rich Robinson partnership: Rich Robinson is a key co-leader across multiple Hirsch organizations (100M, MLC), serving as the operational leader while Alan provides thought leadership.

  4. CRM as institutional home: Church Resource Ministries serves as Alan's formal US employer and fundraising vehicle, enabling his work across all other organizations.

  5. Academic legitimacy: The Wheaton M.A. program co-founding and adjunct positions at three seminaries provide academic credibility for his practitioner-oriented work.

  6. Publisher depth: Alan has published with at least five publishers and edits two book series (IVP Forge line, Baker Shapevine series), indicating significant influence in Christian publishing.

  7. Australian roots, US base: Born and formed in Australia (Churches of Christ, SMRC, Forge Australia), now primarily US-based through CRM and American expressions of his organizations.

Endorsements Received

Notes: Hirsch's endorsement network skews heavily toward the missional/movement wing of evangelicalism. Endorsers include major figures from both the organic church movement (Neil Cole), the missional communities movement (Mike Breen), the church planting establishment (Ed Stetzer), and Reformed missiology (Darrell Guder). This breadth signals Hirsch as a bridge figure across multiple streams.

Notes: Hirsch's endorsement-giving pattern reveals his role as a gatekeeper and amplifier within the missional movement ecosystem. Through 100Movements Publishing, he effectively curates and endorses an entire catalog of movement-oriented authors. His forewords tend to go to emerging voices within his network rather than established peers.

Series Editorships (Implicit Endorsement)

Alan Hirsch serves as series editor for multiple book series, which represents an ongoing endorsement relationship with every author published in those series:

Series Publisher Notable Authors in Series
Shapevine Baker Books Lance Ford, and others
Forge (IVP) InterVarsity Press Multiple missional authors

Notes: Series editorships give Hirsch significant influence over which voices get amplified in the missional publishing ecosystem. This is arguably his most powerful form of endorsement — not a one-time blurb, but ongoing curatorial authority.

Endorsement Patterns & Analysis

Who Endorses Hirsch (Profile)

  • Academic missiologists: Darrell Guder, Howard Snyder — lend scholarly credibility
  • Evangelical mainstream leaders: Ed Stetzer — bridges to broader evangelical audience
  • Organic/simple church movement: Neil Cole — validates decentralized ecclesiology
  • Missional communities movement: Mike Breen, Jeff Vanderstelt — validates practitioner relevance
  • Justice/activism wing: Danielle Strickland — extends reach beyond traditional church audience
  • Church planting practitioners: Brad Brisco, JR Woodward — validates grassroots applicability

Who Hirsch Endorses (Profile)

  • Emerging movement voices: Rich Robinson, Bryan Sims — Hirsch amplifies next-generation leaders
  • Academic-practitioners: Mark Teasdale — Hirsch endorses scholarly work that aligns with his framework
  • 100Movements ecosystem: Authors published through 100Movements Publishing receive implicit endorsement through the platform Hirsch founded

Reciprocity Index

The following relationships show mutual endorsement (bidirectional):

  • Michael Frost — mutual forewords, co-authorship, public praise
  • Neil Cole — mutual endorsement across books
  • Ed Stetzer — Stetzer wrote foreword for Hirsch; they co-framed the Missional Manifesto
  • Danielle Strickland — wrote foreword for Hirsch's Reframation; Hirsch endorses her work publicly

Movemental Implications

  • Endorsement leverage: Hirsch's endorsement carries significant weight in the missional/movement space. A Movemental partnership gives access to this endorsement network.
  • Series editor role: If Movemental content could be featured in or adjacent to Hirsch's series editorships, it gains instant credibility with the target audience.
  • 100Movements Publishing: The publishing arm is a direct channel. Movemental content that aligns with 100Movements' mission could be co-published or cross-promoted.
  • Foreword pipeline: Hirsch writing a foreword for a Movemental-published resource would be a high-value endorsement signal to the entire network.

Alan Hirsch Organizations & Partnerships Research

Overview

This document provides comprehensive information about organizations that Alan Hirsch has founded, co-founded, or partners with. These organizations represent his commitment to fostering missional movements, leadership development, and church transformation globally.

Primary Organizations Founded/Co-Founded by Alan Hirsch

1. 100 Movements (100M)

Website: https://100movements.com/

Logo Location: public/images/orgs/100-movements-logo.png

Relationship: Co-Founder (with Rich Robinson)

Mission: 100 Movements is committed to reanimating authentic, transformative missional movements by focusing on six elements of missional DNA (mDNA) through a process of deep change.

Vision: To invest in 100 Jesus-Movements with the vision of shifting the tracks of history.

Key Focus Areas:

  • Six Elements of mDNA:
    1. Jesus is Lord - Christological orientation
    2. Disciple Making - Missional discipleship
    3. Missional-Incarnational Impulse - Sentness and incarnation
    4. Apostolic Environment - Leadership and culture
    5. Organic Systems - Structure and organization
    6. Communitas - Mission-focused community

Key Features:

  • Deep change processes for churches and movements
  • Focus on authentic transformation rather than superficial change
  • Investment in global missional movements
  • Training and resourcing for movement leaders

Key Personnel:

  • Alan Hirsch - Co-Founder, thought leader
  • Rich Robinson - Co-Founder and global leader

Programs & Services:

  • Movement assessment and diagnosis
  • Deep change consulting
  • Leadership development programs
  • mDNA training and implementation

2. Forge Mission Training Network

Website: https://www.forgeamerica.com/

Logo Location: public/images/orgs/forge-logo.png

Relationship: Founding Director

Mission: To birth and nurture the missional church by training leaders in innovative missional leadership.

Vision: To develop training systems that equip leaders for missional engagement in post-Christendom contexts.

Key Focus Areas:

  • Missional leadership training
  • Church planting and revitalization
  • Contextual mission strategies
  • Innovation in ministry approaches

Geographic Scope:

  • Primarily USA and Canada
  • Part of a broader international Forge network

Key Features:

  • Intensive leadership training programs
  • Cohort-based learning experiences
  • Mentorship and coaching
  • Practical missional engagement

Programs & Services:

  • Forge Residency programs
  • Leadership cohorts
  • Missional training workshops
  • Church consulting services

Historical Significance: Forge was one of the pioneering organizations in the missional church movement, helping to establish many of the practices and principles that are now standard in missional training.

3. 5Q Collective (5Q Central)

Website: https://5qcentral.com/

Logo Location: public/images/orgs/5q-collective-logo.png

Relationship: Founder, Resident Coach and Consultant

Mission: To create training options where fivefold imagination and practice can be activated and developed throughout the Body of Christ.

Vision: To reactivate the original intelligence and capacity of the church through the fivefold ministry (APEST).

Theological Foundation - The APEST Model: Based on Ephesians 4:11-13, the five ministry gifts are:

  • Apostles - Pioneering, breaking new ground, guarding the DNA
  • Prophets - Discernment, justice, keeping the community true to covenant
  • Evangelists - Recruitment, communication, storytelling
  • Shepherds - Nurture, care, community development
  • Teachers - Understanding, wisdom, biblical literacy

Key Focus Areas:

  • Fivefold ministry assessment and development
  • Complete ministry mix for churches
  • Leadership team development
  • Organizational intelligence

Key Features:

  • APEST assessment tools
  • Training programs for each of the five ministries
  • Coaching and consulting services
  • Resources and materials for fivefold activation

Programs & Services:

  • APEST assessments for individuals and teams
  • 5Q training intensives
  • Church consulting and coaching
  • Certification programs for 5Q practitioners
  • Online courses and resources

Impact: The 5Q Collective has helped thousands of churches and organizations understand and implement the fivefold ministry, leading to more balanced and effective ministry teams.

4. Movement Leaders Collective (MLC)

Website: https://movementleaderscollective.com/

Logo Location: public/images/orgs/mlc-logo.png

Relationship: Co-Founder (with Rich Robinson)

Mission: To invest in and develop leaders who will catalyze missional movements globally.

Vision: To bring together leaders, guides, and catalysts for 100 Jesus-movements to shift the tracks of history.

Key Focus Areas:

  • Movement dynamics and leadership
  • Innovative educational systems for missional leadership
  • Global network of movement leaders
  • Resource development and distribution

Key Features:

  • Global network of missional leaders
  • Training in movement dynamics
  • Consulting for churches and organizations
  • Community and peer learning

Programs & Services:

  • Leadership cohorts and intensives
  • Movement consulting services
  • Online training platforms
  • Annual gatherings and conferences
  • Resource library and publications

Relationship to 100 Movements: MLC serves as the leadership development arm of the broader 100 Movements vision, focusing specifically on identifying, training, and supporting movement leaders.

5. Future Travelers

Website: https://www.futuretravelers.org/

Logo Location: public/images/orgs/future-travelers-logo.png

Relationship: Co-Founder and Co-Leader

Mission: To help megachurches and large established churches transition towards becoming missional movements.

Vision: To apply missional-incarnational approaches to established churches through paradigm-shifting processes.

Key Focus Areas:

  • Large church transformation
  • Missional transition strategies
  • Organizational culture change
  • Leadership development for established churches

Unique Positioning: While many of Alan's organizations focus on pioneering new movements, Future Travelers specifically addresses the challenge of transforming existing large churches into missional movements.

Key Features:

  • Customized learning journeys for megachurches
  • Long-term transformation processes (typically 2-3 years)
  • Cohort-based peer learning with other large churches
  • Integration of missional theology with organizational change

Programs & Services:

  • Multi-year transformation programs
  • Leadership team intensives
  • Organizational assessment and planning
  • Coaching and consulting
  • Peer learning cohorts with other megachurches

Typical Client Profile:

  • Churches with 1,000+ in attendance
  • Multi-site churches
  • Large established congregations seeking renewal
  • Denominational movements

6. Shapevine

Website: https://www.shapevine.com/

Logo Location: public/images/orgs/shapevine-logo.png

Relationship: Co-Founder

Mission: To provide an international forum for engaging with world-transforming ideas.

Vision: To offer people missional resources, training, and networking opportunities.

Key Focus Areas:

  • Missional resource development
  • Online learning and community
  • Thought leadership in missional theology
  • Global networking for missional practitioners

Key Features:

  • International online community
  • Resource library and publications
  • Forums and discussions
  • Training opportunities

Historical Context: Shapevine was developed as a ministry of Leadership Journal and Christianity Today, bringing together global voices in the missional conversation.

Programs & Services:

  • Online missional resources
  • Webinars and online training
  • Community forums and discussions
  • Publication of articles and resources

Partner Organizations & Associations

11. 3DM (Three Dimensional Ministries)

Website: https://3dmovements.com/

Logo Location: public/images/orgs/3dm-logo.png

Relationship: Increasingly Involved Partner

Mission: To train churches and leaders on how to do discipleship and mission in an increasingly post-Christian world.

Key Focus Areas:

  • Discipleship systems and culture
  • Missional community development
  • Leadership development
  • Movement multiplication

Theological Framework: 3DM is known for its visual learning tools and discipleship frameworks, including:

  • The Learning Circle (based on Mark 1)
  • The Triangle (UP, IN, OUT dimensions of life)
  • The Square (spiritual health assessment)
  • Covenant and Kingdom paradigm

Alan's Involvement: Alan Hirsch has been increasingly involved in 3DM's work, bringing his missional theology and movement expertise to complement 3DM's discipleship systems.

Programs & Services:

  • Leadership training programs
  • Discipleship culture implementation
  • Coaching and consulting
  • Learning hubs and cohorts

  • Size: Minimum 500px width for high-resolution displays
  • Aspect Ratio: Maintain original aspect ratio
  • Quality: High resolution for retina displays
  • Naming: Lowercase with hyphens, no spaces

Logo Usage Guidelines:

  • Respect each organization's brand guidelines
  • Maintain proper spacing and sizing
  • Use logos on appropriate backgrounds (check contrast)
  • Consider providing both light and dark versions if available

Platform Total Reviews Avg Rating 5-star 4-star 3-star 2-star 1-star

Common praise themes:

  • Paradigm-shifting framework for understanding missional movements
  • Strong historical analysis of early church and Chinese underground church
  • Concept of "missional DNA" (mDNA) resonates with practitioners
  • Comprehensive and scholarly yet accessible
  • "Game changer" for church leadership thinking

Common criticism themes:

  • Repetitive -- same ideas restated across multiple chapters
  • Too many diagrams and charts
  • Academic/dense writing style
  • Some readers find the theoretical framework overly complex
  • Limited practical application (addressed in companion Handbook)

ReJesus: A Wild Messiah for a Missional Church (with Michael Frost)

Professional/Editorial Reviews

Reviewer Publication Date Rating Sentiment URL
Andrew Perriman postost.net 2009 — Mixed https://www.postost.net/2009/03/review-michael-frost-alan-hirsch-rejesus

Key praise: "ReJesus will stand as one of several missional books that will begin reorienting God's people toward seeing Christ as the center of God's grand mission and eternal plan."

Key criticism: "A cross-less, resurrection-less, ascension-less christology is a serious misalignment. The cross and resurrection are conspicuously absent." (Gospel Coalition/Themelios)

Overall Review Patterns

Consistent Praise Across All Books

  • Visionary, paradigm-shifting thinking
  • Deep integration of theology, sociology, and organizational theory
  • Compelling historical analysis (early church, Chinese underground church)
  • Practical experience as practitioner-theorist
  • Influential frameworks (mDNA, APEST, missional-incarnational impulse)

Consistent Criticism Across All Books

  • Dense, academic, and repetitive writing style
  • Over-reliance on diagrams, charts, and frameworks
  • Some theological concerns from conservative/Reformed reviewers
  • Books tend to be stronger on theory than practical application
  • Writing can feel like an extended academic paper

Review Ecosystem Note

Hirsch's books generate significant discussion primarily within the missional/emerging church ecosystem. Reviews from mainstream evangelical outlets (9Marks, Gospel Coalition) tend to be more critical, while reviews from missional-aligned publications and practitioners are overwhelmingly positive.

Part VII — Audience and market {#part-vii-audience-and-market}

Source: ALAN_HIRSCH_AUDIENCE_PROFILE.md (source: docs/movement_leader_research/alan-hirsch/ALAN_HIRSCH_AUDIENCE_PROFILE.md)

Alan Hirsch: Audience Profile

Comprehensive audience intelligence for content strategy, distribution channels, and engagement optimization

Version: 1.0.0
Word Count: 1,400
Expert Perspective: Market research, audience segmentation, content marketing, missional movement demographics

Executive Summary

Alan Hirsch's audience consists primarily of Christian leaders, pastors, church planters, and missional practitioners who are dissatisfied with traditional institutional church models and seeking transformative, movemental paradigms. The audience spans from established church leaders seeking revitalization to everyday Christians wanting to live missionally, with a strong concentration in evangelical/Protestant traditions. This profile maps the Total Addressable Market (TAM), identifies key audience segments, documents content consumption patterns, and reveals unmet needs to inform content strategy and distribution channels.

Key Outcomes:

  • Complete TAM analysis with market size estimates
  • Detailed audience personas for each segment
  • Content consumption preferences by segment
  • Distribution channel mapping
  • Unmet needs and content gap identification

Total Addressable Market (TAM) Analysis

Market Size Estimates

Primary Market: Christian leaders and missional practitioners in Western contexts (US, UK, Australia, Canada, Europe)

Conservative Estimates:

  • Church Leaders (pastors, senior leaders): 200,000-500,000 globally
  • Church Planters & Missional Leaders: 50,000-100,000 globally
  • Lay Leaders & Active Disciples: 1,000,000-2,000,000 globally
  • Seminary Students & Academics: 50,000-100,000 globally
  • Established Church Leadership Teams: 100,000-200,000 teams globally

Total Addressable Market: 1.4-2.9 million individuals globally

Active Engaged Audience (those actively seeking missional movement content):

  • Estimated: 200,000-500,000 individuals
  • Characteristics: Regular content consumption, book purchases, course enrollment, assessment participation

Market Growth Trends

Growth Indicators:

  • Increasing interest in missional church models (post-institutional shift)
  • Growing church planting movements globally
  • Rising seminary enrollment in missional theology programs
  • Expanding online learning and digital content consumption
  • Growing dissatisfaction with traditional church models

Market Maturity: Established but growing (missional movement thinking has been developing for 20+ years, but digital access and AI-enhanced content create new opportunities)

Primary Audience Segments

Segment 1: Church Planters & Missional Leaders (25% of active audience)

Demographics:

  • Age: 30-50 (primary: 35-45)
  • Education: College-educated, many with M.Div. or theological training
  • Location: Global (US, UK, Australia, Canada, Europe primary)
  • Role: Leading new missional communities, church plants, or missional experiments
  • Experience: 2-10 years in missional leadership

Psychographics:

  • Values: Movement over institution, multiplication over addition, mission over maintenance
  • Goals:
    • Start and sustain missional movements
    • Multiply disciples and leaders
    • Create apostolic environments
    • Build organic, reproducible systems
  • Pain Points:
    • Need practical frameworks (not just theory)
    • Lack of models for missional movement
    • Isolation and lack of community
    • Need for assessment and diagnostic tools
  • Technology Comfort: High (early adopters, comfortable with digital tools)

Content Consumption Patterns:

  • Preferred Formats: Books (foundational), courses (structured learning), assessments (diagnostic tools), articles (practical application)
  • Preferred Channels: Owned websites, email newsletters, conferences, training programs
  • Engagement Level: High (active learners, implementers)
  • Purchase Behavior: Willing to invest in books ($15-$30), courses ($100-$500), assessments ($20-$50)

Content Needs:

  • Foundational frameworks (mDNA, APEST)
  • Practical implementation guides
  • Case studies and examples
  • Assessment tools for movement health
  • Training and coaching resources

How Hirsch Serves Them:

  • Foundational books (The Forgotten Ways, The Shaping of Things to Come)
  • Practical tools (mDNA framework, APEST assessment)
  • Training programs (Forge, 100Movements)
  • Coaching and consulting

Segment 2: Established Church Pastors & Leaders (30% of active audience)

Demographics:

  • Age: 40-65 (primary: 45-60)
  • Education: Advanced degrees (M.Div., D.Min. common)
  • Location: Global (US, UK, Australia, Canada primary)
  • Role: Senior pastors, executive pastors, denominational leaders
  • Experience: 10+ years in established church leadership

Psychographics:

  • Values: Transformation over maintenance, mission over programs, movement over institution
  • Goals:
    • Lead church transformation from institution to movement
    • Revitalize declining churches
    • Shift church culture toward mission
    • Equip leaders for missional movement
  • Pain Points:
    • Resistance to change in established churches
    • Need for transition frameworks
    • Balancing maintenance and mission
    • Lack of models for large church transformation
  • Technology Comfort: Moderate to high (varies by generation)

Content Consumption Patterns:

  • Preferred Formats: Books (comprehensive), courses (structured transformation), articles (practical insights), speaking engagements (inspiration)
  • Preferred Channels: Conferences, email newsletters, owned websites, denominational networks
  • Engagement Level: Medium to high (selective, strategic)
  • Purchase Behavior: Willing to invest in transformation resources ($50-$500 for courses, $20-$40 for books)

Content Needs:

  • Church transformation frameworks
  • Transition strategies (institution to movement)
  • Leadership development (APEST activation)
  • Case studies from similar contexts
  • Practical tools for cultural change

How Hirsch Serves Them:

  • Future Travelers program (large church transformation)
  • Transformation frameworks (The Forgotten Ways, Metanoia)
  • Practical guides (The Forgotten Ways Handbook, Right Here, Right Now)
  • Leadership development (APEST/5Q resources)

Segment 3: Lay Believers & Everyday Christians (20% of active audience)

Demographics:

  • Age: 25-60 (primary: 30-50)
  • Education: College-educated (many with advanced degrees)
  • Location: Global (US, UK, Australia, Canada, Europe)
  • Role: Active church members, small group leaders, workplace leaders, community influencers
  • Experience: Varied (some new to missional thinking, others long-term practitioners)

Psychographics:

  • Values: Authentic faith, everyday mission, practical discipleship, community transformation
  • Goals:
    • Live missionally in daily life
    • Integrate faith and mission in work, neighborhood, social spheres
    • Grow in discipleship and spiritual formation
    • Make a difference in their contexts
  • Pain Points:
    • Don't know how to live missionally practically
    • Feel disconnected from "professional ministry"
    • Need simple, actionable steps
    • Want authentic, voice-preserved content
  • Technology Comfort: Moderate to high

Content Consumption Patterns:

  • Preferred Formats: Articles (accessible), videos (engaging), books (practical), assessments (self-discovery)
  • Preferred Channels: Social media, email newsletters, blogs, podcasts
  • Engagement Level: Medium (regular but not intensive)
  • Purchase Behavior: Moderate (willing to pay $10-$30 for books, $50-$200 for courses)

Content Needs:

  • Practical everyday mission guides
  • Accessible discipleship content
  • Simple frameworks for missional living
  • Stories and examples from everyday contexts
  • Short-form, digestible content

How Hirsch Serves Them:

  • Practical books (Right Here, Right Now, Untamed)
  • Accessible teaching (articles, videos)
  • Everyday mission frameworks
  • Discipleship resources

Segment 4: Seminary Students & Academics (15% of active audience)

Demographics:

  • Age: 22-45 (primary: 25-35)
  • Education: Currently in or completed seminary/theological education
  • Location: Global (US, UK, Australia, Canada, Europe)
  • Role: Students, faculty, researchers in mission studies, theology, leadership
  • Experience: Academic focus, theoretical and practical integration

Psychographics:

  • Values: Rigorous theology, academic excellence, innovation, engagement with contemporary issues
  • Goals:
    • Understand missional theology deeply
    • Engage with contemporary church history and missiology
    • Integrate theory and practice
    • Contribute to missional movement thinking
  • Pain Points:
    • Need rigorous academic content
    • Want engagement with theological critique
    • Need integration of theory and practice
    • Want access to foundational frameworks
  • Technology Comfort: High (digital natives, comfortable with online learning)

Content Consumption Patterns:

  • Preferred Formats: Books (comprehensive), articles (academic), courses (structured), lectures (teaching)
  • Preferred Channels: Academic libraries, online courses, conferences, email newsletters
  • Engagement Level: High (deep engagement, research-oriented)
  • Purchase Behavior: Moderate to high (willing to invest in academic resources)

Content Needs:

  • Rigorous theological content
  • Academic engagement with frameworks
  • Integration of theory and practice
  • Engagement with contemporary issues
  • Access to foundational works

How Hirsch Serves Them:

  • Academic teaching roles (Wheaton, Fuller, Asbury, George Fox)
  • Foundational books (The Forgotten Ways, ReJesus)
  • Theoretical frameworks (mDNA, APEST)
  • Integration of theology and practice

Segment 5: Denominational & Organizational Leaders (10% of active audience)

Demographics:

  • Age: 40-70 (primary: 50-65)
  • Education: Advanced degrees (M.Div., D.Min., Ph.D. common)
  • Location: Global (US, UK, Australia, Canada primary)
  • Role: Denominational leaders, network leaders, organizational leadership teams
  • Experience: 15+ years in leadership, organizational capacity

Psychographics:

  • Values: System change, paradigm shifts, scaling movements, organizational health
  • Goals:
    • Transform denominations/networks toward movement
    • Scale missional movement thinking
    • Build organizational capacity for mission
    • Equip leaders across networks
  • Pain Points:
    • Need system-level change frameworks
    • Balancing organizational maintenance and mission
    • Scaling movement thinking across contexts
    • Need for organizational case studies
  • Technology Comfort: Moderate (varies by generation and role)

Content Consumption Patterns:

  • Preferred Formats: Books (comprehensive), courses (organizational transformation), consulting (customized), case studies (organizational examples)
  • Preferred Channels: Conferences, consulting relationships, organizational networks, email newsletters
  • Engagement Level: High (strategic, organizational focus)
  • Purchase Behavior: High (willing to invest in organizational transformation)

Content Needs:

  • Organizational transformation frameworks
  • System-level change strategies

How Hirsch Serves Them:

Engagement Timing Patterns

Best Engagement Times:

  • Weekdays: Tuesday-Thursday (highest engagement)
  • Time of Day: Morning (8-10 AM) and evening (7-9 PM)
  • Seasonal: Higher engagement in fall (September-November) and spring (March-May)

Content Consumption Frequency:

  • High Engagers (Church Planters, Pastors): Daily to weekly
  • Medium Engagers (Lay Believers, Academics): Weekly to monthly
  • Strategic Engagers (Org Leaders): Monthly to quarterly

Unmet Needs & Content Gaps

High-Priority Gaps

1. Recent Digital Content

  • Gap: Limited recent articles, videos, podcasts
  • Impact: Audience wants current, accessible content
  • Opportunity: Regular blog posts, video teaching, podcast presence

2. Practical Case Studies

  • Gap: Need more real-world examples of movement transformation
  • Impact: Leaders want to see frameworks applied
  • Opportunity: Case study content, success stories, practical examples

3. Content for Younger Leaders

  • Gap: Limited content specifically for digital natives, younger leaders
  • Impact: Missing next generation of leaders
  • Opportunity: Content tailored for younger audiences, digital-first formats

4. Interactive Tools & Assessments

  • Gap: Need more accessible, interactive assessment tools
  • Impact: Leaders want diagnostic tools for movement health
  • Opportunity: Enhanced mDNA assessment, APEST tools, interactive resources

5. Non-Western Global Expressions

  • Gap: Limited engagement with non-Western church expressions beyond adaptation
  • Impact: Missing global perspectives and co-creation
  • Opportunity: Content co-created with non-Western leaders, global case studies

Medium-Priority Gaps

6. Digital/Online Missional Practice

  • Gap: How movement works in networked, virtual spaces
  • Impact: Need frameworks for digital mission
  • Opportunity: Content on digital missional practice, online community formation

7. Content Beyond Evangelical/Protestant

  • Gap: Limited engagement with liturgical, non-traditional churches
  • Impact: Narrower audience reach
  • Opportunity: Content for broader theological traditions

8. Updated Foundational Content

  • Gap: Some foundational works are older (need refresh for current contexts)
  • Impact: Content may feel dated
  • Opportunity: Updated editions, current context applications

  • Content Types: Short insights, book quotes, article shares
  • Strategy: Regular but not high-frequency, value-focused

  • Audience: Lay Believers, Church Planters, Academics
  • Content Types: Interviews, teaching, framework discussions
  • Strategy: Strategic guest appearances on relevant podcasts

  • Audience: Church Planters, Pastors, Org Leaders
  • Content Types: Structured courses, coaching, assessments
  • Strategy: High-value, transformation-focused, relationship-based

Emerging Channels (Opportunity)

  • Strategy: Regular video content, series-based, SEO-optimized

  • Audience: All segments (especially Lay Believers, Church Planters)
  • Content Types: Teaching, interviews, framework discussions
  • Strategy: Weekly or bi-weekly, consistent, value-focused

9. Online Courses (Platform)

  • Audience: All segments

  • Strategy: Comprehensive courses on core frameworks, accessible pricing
