Estimated tier: Established (50K-250K)

Estimated total reach: ~150,000-200,000 unique individuals across all channels

Rationale: Social media following (~59K combined) understates his actual influence. The 150,000+ APEST assessments indicate a far larger engaged audience. His books have sold significantly more than the ~16,762 Goodreads ratings suggest (Goodreads ratings typically represent 1-5% of total readers). Seminary adoption at 4 institutions, conference speaking to 50,000+ cumulative attendees, and 20 books across 7 publishers over 20+ years point to an audience that extends well beyond his direct digital footprint. His audience is disproportionately influential (pastors, denominational leaders, seminary professors) rather than numerically massive.

Audience Profile

Who Is the Audience?

Primary audience: Church leaders (senior pastors, lead pastors, executive pastors) of mid-to-large Protestant churches in the US, Australia, UK, and Canada who are questioning the attractional/institutional model and seeking frameworks for missional transformation. Typically ages 35-55, seminary-educated, leading churches of 200-2,000+ members.

Secondary audience: Church planters, missional community leaders, and denominational strategists actively building new expressions of church. Younger (28-45), often bi-vocational, connected through networks like Exponential, Forge, Verge, and Fresh Expressions. They use Hirsch's frameworks (mDNA, APEST) as practical tools for team-building and organizational design.

Tertiary audience: Seminary students and academic missiologists studying missional ecclesiology, movement theory, and church leadership. They encounter Hirsch through required reading at Wheaton, Fuller, Asbury, and George Fox seminaries, and through academic citations. Also includes lay leaders and "missionally curious" Christians who discovered Hirsch through Goodreads, podcast appearances, or recommendations from their pastor.

Audience Demographics (Estimated)

Dimension Assessment
Primary role Pastor / Church planter / Denominational leader / Seminary student
Age range 30-60 (core: 35-50)
Education High -- majority have seminary education (MDiv, DMin, MA) or equivalent leadership training
Denominational breadth Broad (multi-tradition) -- spans mainline Protestant, evangelical, Anabaptist, Pentecostal/charismatic, Anglican, and non-denominational. Evidence: speaks at Mennonite USA, Foursquare Connection, Churches of Christ, Anglican (Redeemer City to City), and non-denominational (Exponential).
Geographic concentration US-centric with significant international presence (Australia, UK, Canada, New Zealand)
Gender split Estimated 65-75% male based on conference demographics and pastoral leadership demographics in his primary audience. Women are present particularly through Debra Hirsch collaborations and Missio Alliance / Fresh Expressions channels.

Audience Personas

Persona 1: Marcus -- Senior Pastor Seeking Transformation

Field Value
Role Senior pastor of a 600-member evangelical church (suburban US)
Age 44
Education MDiv from a major evangelical seminary
Tradition Non-denominational evangelical, formerly Southern Baptist
Discovery path Recommended The Forgotten Ways by a church planting friend; later encountered Hirsch at Exponential conference
Primary format Books first, then conference talks, then podcasts
Use case Seeking frameworks to transition his attractional church toward missional engagement without losing the congregation
Pain point Church is "successful" by traditional metrics (attendance, budget) but feels spiritually stagnant; senses that programs and Sunday services alone are not producing disciples

What Marcus values in Hirsch: The mDNA and APEST frameworks give him a theological vocabulary and diagnostic tool for what feels wrong. The concept that the church is fundamentally a movement, not an institution, validates his instinct that something needs to change. He appreciates that Hirsch is not anti-church but pro-transformation.

What Marcus struggles with: Hirsch's books can feel academic and repetitive. Marcus wants more practical "how-to" steps. He appreciates the Forgotten Ways Handbook and Activating 5Q more than the theoretical works. He worries about how to implement these ideas without alienating his elder board.

Persona 2: Anika -- Church Planter and Forge Alumna

Field Value
Role Bi-vocational church planter leading a missional community network in an urban neighborhood
Age 33
Education MA in Missional Church Movements (Wheaton College -- the program Hirsch co-founded)
Tradition Anabaptist-influenced, currently non-denominational
Discovery path Encountered Hirsch's work in seminary; attended Forge residency; took APEST assessment
Primary format Podcasts and training cohorts; uses books as reference texts for her community
Use case Applying APEST framework to team development; using mDNA as a diagnostic for her network of house churches
Pain point Feels isolated as a female leader in a space that is predominantly male; struggles to find funding for bi-vocational missional work; needs practical tools more than theory

What Anika values in Hirsch: APEST gives her a language for why her team needs diversity of gifts. The incarnational-missional framework validates her grassroots, neighborhood-embedded approach. Forge and 5Q training provided her with a community of practitioners.

What Anika struggles with: The cost and access barriers to 5Q training and Forge programs. She wishes Hirsch's content was more accessible digitally (no YouTube channel, no free online course). She also wishes for more voices from women and people of color within Hirsch's ecosystem.

Persona 3: Dr. James -- Seminary Professor and Movement Researcher

Field Value
Role Associate professor of practical theology at an evangelical seminary
Age 52
Education PhD in Missiology
Tradition Reformed evangelical (PCA)
Discovery path Encountered The Shaping of Things to Come through academic circles in the mid-2000s; has since assigned Hirsch in multiple courses
Primary format Books (primarily); academic articles; conference presentations
Use case Assigns Hirsch alongside Newbigin, Bosch, and Guder in missiology courses; uses APEST as a case study in ecclesiology; cites Hirsch in published papers
Pain point Appreciates Hirsch's synthesis but finds some theological claims under-argued (especially APEST as "divine intelligence"); wishes for more rigorous engagement with Reformed ecclesiology

What James values in Hirsch: The breadth of synthesis -- Hirsch uniquely bridges sociology, organizational theory, missiology, and biblical theology. The Forgotten Ways and The Permanent Revolution are among the most cited works in contemporary missional ecclesiology. The mDNA framework is genuinely innovative.

What James struggles with: Some theological overreach (e.g., equating APEST with divine attributes in 5Q). The repetition across books. The lack of engagement with critical scholarly responses. He recommends Hirsch to students with caveats about exegetical rigor.

Criticism Frequency Example Quote
Repetitive writing across and within books Very High "A solid book that honestly should be cut in half. It's incredibly repetitive, and he seems to write some chapters simply to unveil yet another chart"
Overly academic/dense for practitioners High "Detailed and technical and requires a good deal of reflection for maximum profit. It is not an easy read"
Too many diagrams and frameworks Medium "Too many diagrams and charts"; "reads as if it were written to make something more complicated"

Engagement Patterns

Most Engaging Content Types

Format Engagement Level Evidence
Books Highest 16,762 Goodreads ratings across 92 editions; 20 books across 7 publishers; The Forgotten Ways widely described as "paradigm-shifting" and "the most influential book on missional church"
Speaking (conferences) Very High Recurring speaker at Exponential (6+ years), Verge (3 years), Missio Alliance; 50,000+ cumulative audience; audiences 2,000-5,000+ per conference
Assessments (APEST/5Q) Very High 150,000+ APEST tests taken; assessments drive ongoing engagement with 5Q ecosystem
Podcast (guest) High 28+ guest appearances across 22+ podcasts; consistent invitations 2020-2025 indicate sustained demand
Training/courses Moderate-High Multiple platforms (Ephesiology, 5Q Central, Forge Teachable); paid masterclasses; Wheaton MA program
Blog/Substack Moderate 1,500 Substack subscribers; blog on alanhirsch.org with categorized archives; guest posts on other Substacks
Social media Low-Moderate Twitter ~37.9K but engagement is typical of niche thought-leaders; used primarily to amplify content published elsewhere
Video Low (own) / Moderate (third-party) No personal YouTube channel; 38+ videos distributed across 20+ third-party channels; no centralized video presence

Topics That Drive Engagement

Topic Engagement Platform Evidence
APEST / 5Q / Fivefold ministry Very High All platforms 150,000+ assessments; ~30% of podcast topics; 3 dedicated books; Exponential pre-conferences
Missional movements / mDNA / The Forgotten Ways Very High Books, conferences Flagship book (4.08 rating, 1,581 ratings); "most influential book on missional church"; conference keynote staple
Discipleship vs. consumerism High Video, conferences Most prominent video ("Disciple Making vs. Consumerism" on ChurchLeaders); recurring conference theme
Communitas and liminality Medium-High Podcasts, books Recent podcast focus (Fresh Expressions 2024); resonates with post-COVID church leaders
Metanoia / organizational transformation Medium Books, conferences 2023 book launch at Awakenings; plenary talk; appeals to leaders navigating institutional change
Christology and ReJesus Medium Books, podcasts Co-authored with Frost; controversial among Reformed critics but resonant with missional practitioners
Church multiplication and microchurch Growing Podcasts, consulting Aligns with post-COVID trend toward smaller, decentralized expressions

Movemental Audience Implications

  • Content format priority: Books and structured courses should be the anchor content on the platform. Hirsch's audience is accustomed to deep, framework-driven content and will pay for it. The 150,000+ APEST assessment users represent a built-in audience for digital training products. Video content is a major gap -- centralizing his dispersed video footprint (38+ videos across 20+ channels) onto Movemental would provide immediate differentiation.

  • Topic focus: APEST/5Q content should be the lead offering -- it has the broadest appeal, the most engaged audience (150K+ assessments), and the most practical application. The Forgotten Ways / mDNA framework is the prestige content that establishes credibility. Discipleship and organizational transformation (Metanoia) content should follow. Communitas/liminality is a growing topic that resonates with post-COVID church leaders.

  • Cross-pollination: Michael Frost shares the largest audience overlap and would be the highest-value companion author on the platform. Brad Brisco (if already on Movemental) would cross-link naturally through Forge and Exponential networks. Neil Cole, Hugh Halter, and Alan Roxburgh share significant audience segments. Dave Ferguson bridges into the megachurch/multisite audience that Hirsch alone does not fully reach.

  • Growth opportunity: The biggest growth opportunity is digital -- Hirsch's audience is underserved online. No personal YouTube channel, no major free online course, no centralized video library. The 150,000+ APEST assessment users are a warm audience that could be converted to platform subscribers. International growth (UK, Australia, New Zealand, South Africa) is also significant given his origins and existing conference relationships in those markets.

  • Monetization signal: Strong. Hirsch's audience already pays for content: books across 7 publishers, paid masterclasses on Ephesiology, paid Forge residencies, paid APEST assessments, conference registration fees ($200-500+), and consulting engagements with megachurches (Future Travelers). Seminary students pay tuition for programs he teaches in. The audience is comprised of church leaders with organizational budgets who regularly invest in leadership development. The 150,000+ APEST assessment completions at paid or registration-gated access is the strongest monetization signal -- this audience is accustomed to paying for frameworks and tools, not just consuming free content.

Part VIII — Strategic analysis and Movemental fit {#part-viii-strategic-analysis-and-movemental-fit}

Source: analysis/content-analysis.md (source: docs/movement_leader_research/alan-hirsch/analysis/content-analysis.md)

Content Analysis: Alan Hirsch

Executive Summary

Alan Hirsch is the foremost theorist of apostolic movement in the contemporary Western church. His distinctive contribution is the articulation of a phenomenology of Jesus movements -- naming the latent "Apostolic Genius" (mDNA) encoded in every Christian community and providing a systematic framework for reactivating it. Across 20 books, 30+ articles, 38+ videos, and 7 training programs spanning 2003-2024, Hirsch has built an integrated intellectual architecture that moves from Christology (Jesus is Lord) through missiology (incarnational mission) to ecclesiology (the church as movement). His second major contribution -- the APEST/5Q framework -- has become one of the most widely adopted models for understanding fivefold ministry, with over 150,000 assessments taken globally. No other thinker has so comprehensively integrated movement theory, organizational science, and missional theology into a single coherent system.

Theme Map

Primary Themes (Core to their identity)

Theme Description Key Works Centrality
Apostolic Genius / mDNA The six-element operating system of Jesus movements: Jesus is Lord, Disciple Making, Missional-Incarnational Impulse, APEST, Organic Systems, Liminality-Communitas The Forgotten Ways (2006/2016), The Forgotten Ways Handbook (2009), On the Verge (2011) 10
APEST / 5Q (Fivefold Ministry) The five ministry callings from Ephesians 4:11 as the "original intelligence and capacity" of the Body of Christ -- not just leadership roles but the theo-genetic code of the church 5Q (2017), The Permanent Revolution (2012), Activating 5Q (2018), The Permanent Revolution Playbook (2014) 10
Missional-Incarnational Impulse The church follows the pattern of the Incarnation: going out (missional) and going deep (incarnational) -- embedding in culture rather than extracting from it The Shaping of Things to Come (2003/2013), Right Here, Right Now (2011), Untamed (2010) 9
Jesus is Lord (Christological Center) The primal confession that gates everything -- Christology determines missiology, missiology determines ecclesiology ReJesus (2008/2022), The Forgotten Ways ch. 4, Untamed (2010) 9
Movement vs. Institution The fundamental paradigm distinction: the NT church was a movement, not an institution; institutionalization is deviation, not maturation Throughout all works; central to The Forgotten Ways, On the Verge, The Starfish and the Spirit (2021) 9

Secondary Themes (Recurring but not defining)

Theme Description Key Works Centrality
Disciple Making (vs. Consumerism) The church makes disciples, not consumers; discipleship is the core task from which everything else flows Disciplism (2014), Untamed (2010), The Forgotten Ways ch. 5, Verge talks 8
Liminality & Communitas Authentic community is forged through shared ordeal and risk, not comfort and programs; borrowed from Victor Turner The Faith of Leap (2011/2025), The Forgotten Ways ch. 7, Fresh Expressions podcast (2024) 7
Organic Systems & Decentralization Simple, reproducible, networked structures that multiply like viruses rather than centralized institutions that control The Starfish and the Spirit (2021), The Forgotten Ways ch. 9 7
Post-Christendom Transition The Western church has lost its cultural privilege and must learn to be missional rather than attractional The Shaping of Things to Come (2003), On the Verge (2011), Saturate articles 6
Hebraic vs. Hellenistic Thinking The Western church is distorted by Greek dualism (sacred/secular, spiritual/physical); recovery of holistic Hebraic modes is essential ReJesus (2008), Reframation (2019), Untamed (2010) 6

Emerging Themes (Recent or developing)

Theme Description Evidence Direction
Metanoia / Deep Transformation Repentance as paradigm-level cognitive-spiritual revolution -- how individuals, churches, and organizations actually change from the inside out Metanoia (2023), Awakenings 2023 plenary Deepening the interior dimension of transformation; integrating social science with theology of change
Reframation / Re-enchantment Changing mental frameworks to transform perception of God, people, and mission; recovery of wonder, poetry, and biblical imagination Reframation (2019), LAUNCH podcast (2020) Moving from structural critique to imaginative renewal; influence of C.S. Lewis and Hans Urs von Balthasar
Kingdom Potential in Disruption Navigating change and unlocking Kingdom potential in a post-COVID, post-stable world All Change (2024, with Rich Robinson) Applying movement principles to a world in perpetual disruption
Digital Church Movements How APEST and movement dynamics translate to digital contexts The Church Digital podcast EP300 (2024) Early exploration; not yet a major written theme

Named Frameworks & Models

mDNA (Missional DNA)

Field Value
Introduced in The Forgotten Ways (2006)
What it describes The six interdependent elements that constitute "Apostolic Genius" -- the operating system of Jesus movements
Components (1) Jesus is Lord, (2) Disciple Making, (3) Missional-Incarnational Impulse, (4) Apostolic Environment (APEST), (5) Organic Systems, (6) Liminality-Communitas. The Holy Spirit is the atmosphere in which all six operate.
Visual Yes (hexagonal diagram with "Jesus is Lord" at center; each element interconnected)
Adoption Widely cited -- adopted by Exponential, Forge, Fresh Expressions, Forming Lutherans, Tampa Underground, and numerous church planting networks globally

How it works: Like biological DNA, mDNA is found in every living cell of the church, is transferable, self-replicating, and carries vital information for healthy reproduction. "Jesus is Lord" gates overall maturity -- no element can rise above the church's Christological depth. When all six are present and interacting, Apostolic Genius emerges as a system greater than the sum of its parts. When one or more are suppressed (as in institutional Christendom), the system defaults to maintenance mode. The task of leadership is to create environments where the dormant code can be reactivated. Hirsch argues: "The possibility of movement lies at the heart of every church" -- the code is already there.

APEST / 5Q

Field Value
Introduced in The Forgotten Ways (2006) as one mDNA element; developed fully in The Permanent Revolution (2012) and 5Q (2017)
What it describes The fivefold ministry pattern from Ephesians 4:11 as the "original intelligence and capacity" of the Body of Christ
Components Apostles (extend/guard), Prophets (covenant faithfulness), Evangelists (recruit/communicate), Shepherds (nurture/protect), Teachers (understand/transmit)
Visual Yes (multiple diagrams -- pentagonal model, hand metaphor, body systems analogy)
Adoption Widely cited -- 150,000+ APEST assessments taken through 5Q Central; adopted by denominations (LMC Anabaptist Churches, Churches of Christ), Exponential network, Redeemer City to City, multiple seminaries

How it works: Christ himself perfectly embodies all five functions. The ascended Christ distributes these callings to the entire Body -- not just leaders -- so the church can grow into maturity. Western churches have typically reduced ministry to Shepherd-Teacher (the "ST" default), effectively "exiling" Apostolic, Prophetic, and Evangelistic functions. This truncation produces a maintenance-oriented church incapable of movement. Reactivating all five creates what Hirsch calls "5Q" -- a measure of ecclesial intelligence analogous to IQ or EQ. When all five are present and integrated, the church has the environment for missional movement. The framework has evolved from a leadership model (2006) to a comprehensive ecclesiology with its own assessment tools, training programs, and organizational applications (2017-present).

Christology > Missiology > Ecclesiology

Field Value
Introduced in ReJesus (2008/2009, with Michael Frost)
What it describes A cascading theological sequence: who Jesus is determines how we do mission, which determines what the church looks like
Components Three-step logical chain; get the first wrong, everything downstream distorts
Visual Yes (arrow diagram: Christology -> Missiology -> Ecclesiology)
Adoption Widely cited in missional theology circles; referenced by Ed Stetzer, Missio Alliance, Verge Network

How it works: Most churches start with ecclesiology (what kind of church do we want?) and try to fit mission and Jesus into it. Hirsch inverts this: start with Jesus, let Jesus shape your mission, let mission shape your church. "Those who start with the Church and then try to fit mission into it will never find mission. Those who start with mission and then ask what kind of Church is needed to fulfill it will find the Church will be found." This framework is the theological engine beneath the mDNA and APEST systems.

Metanoia (Paradigm-Level Transformation)

Field Value
Introduced in Metanoia (2023, with Rob Kelly)
What it describes Repentance understood as fundamental paradigm shift -- not merely moral reform but cognitive-spiritual revolution that transforms individuals, churches, and organizations from the inside out
Components Mind (paradigm), Soul (pathos/culture), Will (practice) -- "wholehearted metanoia" engages all three
Visual Yes (three-dimensional model of transformation)
Adoption Emerging -- featured at Missio Alliance Awakenings 2023; growing uptake in church leadership circles

How it works: Hirsch argues that Jesus' first public word -- "metanoia" (repent) -- is far more radical than the Western church has understood. It means a complete reorientation of consciousness. Human transformation requires the uniting of mind, soul, and will in a single act of turning. For organizations, this means addressing paradigm (how we think), platform (the culture/soul of the organization), and practice (what we actually do). Metanoia is both instantaneous and ongoing. This framework complements the earlier structural focus of mDNA/APEST by addressing the interior mechanism of how change actually happens.

Reframation

Field Value
Introduced in Reframation (2019, with Mark Nelson)
What it describes The process of changing mental frameworks to transform perception of God, people, and mission -- re-enchanting a disenchanted worldview
Components Drawing on C.S. Lewis, Martin Buber, Hans Urs von Balthasar; calls for "remystifying, repoeticizing, and remythologizing"
Visual No (more literary/philosophical than diagrammatic)
Adoption Niche but growing -- particularly resonant with post-evangelical and creative audiences

How it works: The Western world has undergone a process of disenchantment -- reducing reality to the measurable and the material. The church has participated in this reduction, losing the capacity for wonder, imagination, and encounter. Reframation is the deliberate process of re-enchanting our frames -- seeing God as bigger, people as more sacred (imago Dei), and mission as more beautiful than our current paradigms allow. It represents Hirsch's most literary and philosophical work, marking a shift from organizational frameworks toward imaginative renewal.

Movemental Thinking (Movement vs. Institution)

Field Value
Introduced in Throughout all works; the term "movemental" coined by Hirsch
What it describes The fundamental paradigm distinction between institution (centralized, controlling, maintenance-focused) and movement (organic, multiplicative, network-structured, virus-like)
Components Not a formal model but a pervasive lens; characteristics include: grassroots networks, adaptability, viral reproduction, mission as organizing principle, edge-of-chaos dynamics
Visual Yes (multiple -- starfish vs. spider, life cycle of movements, network diagrams)
Adoption Widely cited -- "movemental" has entered the vocabulary of missional practitioners; the Movemental AI platform takes its name from this concept

How it works: Hirsch argues that the NT church was a movement, not an institution. Movements spread like viruses through decentralized networks; institutions centralize and control. The church's default drift is from movement to institution -- from the "edge of chaos" (where innovation happens) to equilibrium (which, in living systems, is death). Leadership's task is to maintain movement dynamics: simplicity, reproducibility, apostolic sending, networked structure, and mission as organizing principle. "Phenomenal Jesus movements grow precisely because they do not have centralized institutions to block growth through control."

Intellectual Genealogy

Upstream Influences (Who shaped them)

Thinker Influence Area How
Lesslie Newbigin Missional theology, post-Christendom missiology Foundational influence on understanding the West as a mission field; missio Dei framework; the church as sign/instrument/foretaste of the Kingdom
Roland Allen Organic, Spirit-led mission Early 20th-century missionary whose critique of Western methods and advocacy for indigenous church principles directly shaped Hirsch's understanding of apostolic simplicity
Dietrich Bonhoeffer Costly discipleship Influenced Hirsch's theology of discipleship as total-life commitment; "to encounter God is to change"
Victor Turner Liminality and communitas Anthropological concepts adapted for theological use -- the idea that authentic community is forged through shared ordeal, not comfort
Howard Snyder Radical renewal movements Ecclesiology of movements; the "wineskins" metaphor; church history as a succession of renewal movements
David Bosch Comprehensive missiology Transforming Mission paradigm; the integration of diverse missiological traditions
Chinese house church movement Living example of mDNA Studied as the primary contemporary case of Apostolic Genius -- growth from ~2 million to ~120 million in 70 years under persecution
C.S. Lewis Theological imagination Central to Reframation; the re-enchantment of worldview through biblical imagination and literary wonder
Martin Buber Relational philosophy I-Thou encounter; relational theology used in Reframation
Jewish-Hebraic tradition Personal heritage; Shema spirituality Hirsch's own Jewish background informs his critique of Hellenistic dualism and his emphasis on holistic, embodied faith
Karl Jung / Joseph Campbell Archetypes and hero's journey Used in 5Q to argue APEST functions as archetypal/primordial patterns visible across cultures
Paul Tillich Theology of culture Engagement between gospel and culture; theological courage

Exponential Conference / Network Primary intellectual influence on church multiplication movement Recurring plenary speaker 2011-2022; On the Verge was defining document of Exponential 2011
Fresh Expressions movement Intellectual ally; APEST and incarnational mission widely adopted Podcast appearances; cross-pollination of frameworks
Forming Lutherans (LCMS) Direct adoption of mDNA framework for Lutheran missional renewal Multi-part blog series applying Hirsch's mDNA to Lutheran context (2023)

Redeemer City to City (Tim Keller network) Hirsch serves as embedded consultant Applying movement principles to urban church planting globally
NewThing International (Dave Ferguson) Hirsch serves as movement mentor Connecting megachurch multiplication with apostolic movement theory

Intellectual Peers (Contemporary dialogue partners)

Person Relationship Agreement/Tension
Michael Frost Closest intellectual partner; 3 co-authored books; Forge co-founder Deep agreement on missional-incarnational theology; Frost more focused on incarnational practice, Hirsch more on movement systems
Tim Catchim APEST co-developer; 2 co-authored books Strong agreement on APEST; Catchim brings organizational theory expertise
Dave Ferguson Co-author; bridges megachurch and missional worlds Agreement on multiplication; tension between megachurch methodology and organic movement

Scot McKnight Engaged with Hirsch's frameworks at length Mixed -- appreciates missional impulse but questions some theological claims around APEST
Brad Brisco Forge Track co-leader; Movemental platform author Strong agreement on neighborhood-level mission; Brisco applies Hirsch's frameworks to bivocational contexts

Theological Positioning

Dimension Position Evidence
Ecclesiology Low church / Movement -- the church is fundamentally a movement, not an institution; simple, reproducible, organic "The NT church was itself a movement and not an institution" (The Forgotten Ways); founded 7 organizations but critique is always toward organic form
Missiology Incarnational -- "go and be" not "come and see"; mission follows the pattern of the Incarnation The Shaping of Things to Come (2003); "Missional-Incarnational Impulse" as core mDNA element
Leadership APEST -- fivefold distributed ministry; servant-inspirer model, not CEO/hierarchical 5Q (2017); The Permanent Revolution (2012); "authentic APEST authority is inspirational and moral, not positional"
Tradition Post-evangelical with Anabaptist-Restorationist roots -- transcends traditional categories; critiques both liberal and conservative defaults Ordained in Churches of Christ (Australia); draws from Anabaptist, charismatic, Reformed, and Catholic sources
Praxis Integrated but theory-leading -- builds comprehensive frameworks and then develops practical tools The Forgotten Ways (theory) followed by The Forgotten Ways Handbook (practice); 5Q followed by Activating 5Q
Scope Movement / Culture transformation -- thinks beyond local church to global movement dynamics 100Movements vision: "invest in 100 Jesus-Movements to shift the tracks of history"
Engagement Practitioner-theorist -- academic rigor in service of movement catalysis Adjunct at 4 seminaries but not primarily an academic; founded Forge (practitioner training) before writing The Forgotten Ways

Content Evolution

Timeline of Ideas

Period Focus Key Work Shift
1989-2002 (Practitioner phase) Leading SMRC among the marginalized in Melbourne; founding Forge; grassroots incarnational mission No major publications; laboratory phase Ministry practice precedes theory; all later frameworks are grounded in this crucible experience
2003-2006 (Foundational phase) Missional-incarnational theology; mDNA framework; Apostolic Genius The Shaping of Things to Come (2003), The Forgotten Ways (2006) From practice to theory; systematic articulation of what was learned in Melbourne and from studying movements globally
2008-2011 (Expansion phase) Christological center; practical application; movement for established churches ReJesus (2008), The Forgotten Ways Handbook (2009), Untamed (2010), Right Here Right Now (2011), On the Verge (2011), The Faith of Leap (2011) Prolific period; broadening from theory to practical application; bridging to megachurch world via Ferguson partnership
2012-2018 (APEST deepening) APEST as comprehensive framework with assessment tools and organizational applications The Permanent Revolution (2012), 5Q (2017), Activating 5Q (2018) From APEST as one mDNA element to APEST as a standalone comprehensive ecclesiology; founding of 5Q Collective
2018-2023 (Interior turn) How paradigm shifts actually happen; interior transformation; re-enchantment of worldview Reframation (2019), Metanoia (2023) Complementing structural/organizational focus with interior dimensions of transformation; more philosophical and literary register
2024-present (Applied movement) Kingdom potential in disruption; digital movements; movement scaling All Change (2024), 100Movements and MLC activity Applying lifetime frameworks to post-COVID disruption and digital contexts; organizational catalysis over new theory

Has their thinking shifted?

Hirsch's core convictions have been remarkably consistent since 2003: the church is a movement, not an institution; it carries latent apostolic DNA that must be reactivated; Jesus is Lord gates everything. What has evolved is the depth and breadth of the system.

The most significant intellectual development is the trajectory from external to internal. The 2003-2012 phase focused primarily on structures, systems, and organizational paradigms -- what the church should look like and how it should be organized. The 2018-2023 phase turned inward -- how people actually change at the level of cognition, imagination, and will. Metanoia and Reframation represent Hirsch's recognition that structural change without interior transformation is insufficient. The "operating system" needs not just new code (mDNA) but a new processor (metanoia) running on a re-enchanted worldview (reframation).

A secondary evolution is the development of APEST from one element among six (2006) into a comprehensive framework with its own ecosystem of assessments, training, and organizational theory (2017-present). This represents the single framework that has scaled most broadly beyond Hirsch's own audience.

Unique Contribution

What only this author says

  • Apostolic Genius / mDNA as a systematic, named framework: While others have written about missional church, organic church, or movement dynamics, Hirsch is the only thinker who has articulated a comprehensive, named, six-element operating system (mDNA) that integrates Christology, discipleship, mission, leadership, organizational theory, and communal formation into a single coherent model. The concept that every church carries dormant movement DNA that can be "reactivated" is distinctively his.

  • APEST as ecclesial intelligence for all believers, not just leaders: Others have written about fivefold ministry, but Hirsch's distinctive claim is that APEST describes callings latent in every believer (not just leaders) and that the Western church has systematically "exiled" three of the five (Apostle, Prophet, Evangelist), creating a structurally imbalanced institution incapable of movement. The 5Q framework -- measuring ecclesial intelligence like IQ or EQ -- is his unique contribution.

  • "Jesus is Lord" as a system-level governor: The idea that a church's Christological maturity caps the performance of every other dimension of its life -- that if "Jesus is Lord" is at Level 2, the whole mDNA system is capped at Level 2 -- is a distinctive systems-theoretic insight applied to theology.

Where they disagree with peers

Topic Their Position Mainstream Position
APEST scope All five gifts are for all believers, distributed across the entire Body; the church has exiled APE functions Most evangelical traditions limit APEST to leadership offices or argue apostles/prophets ceased (cessationism)
Institution vs. movement Institutionalization is deviation from the apostolic norm, not maturation toward it Mainstream ecclesiology views institutional development as healthy maturation; 9Marks and Reformed traditions defend institutional church
Attractional church model "Come and see" is a Christendom-era artifact; "go and be" (incarnational) is normative Many evangelical and megachurch leaders defend attractional methods as legitimate gospel strategy
Sacred-secular divide Hellenistic dualism has distorted Western Christianity; Hebraic holism is the biblical norm Traditional Reformed and Catholic theology maintains valid sacred-secular distinctions (e.g., two-kingdoms theology)
Apostolic ministry today Active apostolic ministry is essential and ongoing; the church needs apostles now Cessationist traditions argue apostolic office ended with the first century; Reformed traditions argue "apostle" is a completed office

What would be lost without their work

Without Hirsch, the missional church conversation would lack its most comprehensive theoretical architecture. While Newbigin provided the theological foundation and practitioners like Neil Cole provided organic models, Hirsch uniquely bridged theory and practice by creating a systematic, named framework (mDNA/APEST) that could be taught, assessed, and implemented across diverse contexts. The vocabulary of the missional movement -- "missional DNA," "Apostolic Genius," "movemental," "5Q" -- would not exist. The 150,000+ people who have taken the APEST assessment would lack the framework to understand their callings. And the bridge between movement theory and established church practice (via Future Travelers and the Exponential network) would be significantly weaker.

Content Interconnections

How themes connect across works

Hirsch's books form a tightly integrated system, not a disconnected catalog. The architecture works as follows:

  1. Foundation: The Shaping of Things to Come (2003) lays the missional-incarnational groundwork. The Forgotten Ways (2006) builds the comprehensive mDNA/Apostolic Genius system on that foundation.

  2. Christological center: ReJesus (2008) deepens the "Jesus is Lord" element -- the first and governing element of mDNA. Without this book, the mDNA system lacks its theological anchor.

  3. APEST deep dive: The Permanent Revolution (2012) and 5Q (2017) take one element of mDNA -- Apostolic Environment -- and develop it into a comprehensive framework. Activating 5Q (2018) provides the practical toolkit.

  4. Liminality and communitas: The Faith of Leap (2011) develops another mDNA element into a full theology of risk and adventure.

  5. Practical application: The Forgotten Ways Handbook (2009), Right Here, Right Now (2011), and Untamed (2010) translate the theoretical system into everyday practice.

  6. Established church bridge: On the Verge (2011) and The Starfish and the Spirit (2021) apply movement principles to existing large churches and organizational structures.

  7. Interior transformation: Reframation (2019) and Metanoia (2023) address how the paradigm shifts described in earlier books actually happen at the cognitive and spiritual level.

  8. Applied disruption: All Change (2024) applies the lifetime of frameworks to the current moment of cultural disruption.

Recommended reading order

Order Work Why this sequence
1 The Forgotten Ways (2nd ed., 2016) The master framework -- mDNA, Apostolic Genius, the full system. Everything else builds on this.
2 ReJesus (revised, 2022) Deepens the Christological center that governs the entire mDNA system.
3 5Q (2017) The most developed treatment of APEST -- the framework that has scaled most broadly.
4 The Shaping of Things to Come (revised, 2013) The foundational missional-incarnational theology that preceded and informed mDNA.
5 Metanoia (2023) How transformation actually happens -- the interior complement to the structural frameworks.
6 Reframation (2019) Re-enchanting worldview -- the imaginative dimension that structural change alone cannot provide.
7 The Permanent Revolution (2012) For deeper study of apostolic ministry and APEST theory.
8 Untamed (2010) Practical missional discipleship for everyday life.
9 Right Here, Right Now (2011) The most accessible entry point for everyday practitioners.
10 All Change (2024) Current application of all frameworks to a world in disruption.

Movemental Content Strategy Implications

  • Pillar mapping: Hirsch's content serves every major Movemental content pillar: Missional Theology (mDNA, incarnational mission), Leadership Development (APEST/5Q), Organizational Transformation (movement vs. institution, organic systems), Discipleship (disciple making, formation), and Spiritual Formation (metanoia, reframation). He is the most cross-pillar author on the platform.

  • Cross-author connections: Brad Brisco (neighborhood mission, bivocational ministry -- applies Hirsch's frameworks at grassroots level); Michael Frost (incarnational mission, 3 co-authored books -- the closest intellectual partner); Dave Ferguson (church multiplication, megachurch bridge); Neil Cole (organic church, simple church movements); Ed Stetzer (missional ecclesiology, evangelical mainstream). Any author in the missional space will reference or respond to Hirsch.

  • Content gaps: (1) Digital-native expressions of APEST and mDNA -- Hirsch has begun exploring this (Church Digital podcast) but has not written substantively on it. (2) APEST for marketplace/vocational contexts beyond church leadership. (3) Shorter-form, high-accessibility content -- Hirsch's writing tends toward complexity; there is an opportunity for "Hirsch Simplified" content on the platform. (4) Women in APEST -- the framework is gender-neutral in theory but most examples and co-authors are male.

  • Series potential: (1) "The Six Elements of mDNA" -- a 6-part series, one per element, drawing from the book corpus. (2) "APEST in Practice" -- 5-part series on each fivefold calling with assessment integration. (5) "Reading Order" -- a curated learning path through Hirsch's works. (6) "The Forgotten Vocabulary" -- short explainers of Hirsch's coined terms (mDNA, communitas, movemental, Apostolic Genius, reframation).

What Movemental Would Change for This Author

Problems Movemental Solves

  • Content fragmentation: 6+ owned domains (alanhirsch.org, 5qcentral.com, 100movements.org, forgeinternational.com, theforgottenways.org, ephesiology.com) with no unified content experience. Movemental provides a single, discoverable profile connecting all content.
  • Video homelessness: 38+ videos scattered across 20+ third-party channels with no centralized home. Movemental provides a video library with proper organization, search, and recommendation.
  • Assessment dead-end: 150K+ people take the APEST assessment and then... leave. No content journey, no follow-up, no nurture. Movemental can connect assessment results to relevant content (articles, courses, videos) by APEST type.
  • Email conversion failure: 37.9K social followers with only 1.5K email subscribers (4% conversion vs. industry standard 15-25%). Movemental's engagement infrastructure drives email capture and audience ownership.
  • Content siloing: Books exist on Amazon. Articles exist on Saturate. Videos exist on Verge Network. Courses exist on Ephesiology. None are connected. Movemental creates cross-content discovery and recommendation.
  • Invisible embodied work: Seminary teaching, consulting, mentoring, and organizational leadership generate enormous value that never reaches a wider audience. Movemental provides a capture-and-publish pipeline.
  • Monetization gap: Hirsch's intellectual property (frameworks, assessments, courses) is under-monetized compared to peers like Mike Breen (3DM) who have built complete digital ecosystems. Movemental provides course hosting, subscription access, and premium content infrastructure.

Content Movemental Would Surface

  • Full bibliography with structured metadata, reviews, reading paths, and purchase links -- organized by framework (mDNA collection, APEST collection, Christology collection)
  • Video library: All 38+ conference talks, interviews, and podcast appearances in one searchable, categorized collection
  • Audio library: 28+ podcast guest episodes + 5QCast episodes + conference recordings, organized by topic and framework
  • Article archive: 30+ articles from Saturate, Verge Network, Ephesiology, personal blog -- unified and cross-linked
  • Course catalog: Ephesiology masterclasses, 5Q training, Forge residency -- presented as a coherent learning pathway
  • Assessment integration: APEST assessment linked to recommended content based on results
  • Framework explorers: Interactive guides to mDNA (6 elements) and APEST (5 types) with linked content for each
  • Collaboration network: Visual map of co-authors, endorsers, and allies -- with links to their Movemental profiles where they exist
  • Event history and upcoming events: Conference schedule, speaking availability, past talks with recordings

New Content Movemental Would Enable

  • "The Forgotten Ways" course: Structured 8-12 module self-paced course based on mDNA framework, incorporating existing video, new recorded lectures, discussion guides, and assessment checkpoints
  • "APEST for Everyone" course: Accessible introduction to five-fold ministry for individuals and teams, built around the APEST assessment with personalized learning paths
  • Post-assessment content journeys: "You scored as a Prophet -- here's your learning path" with curated articles, videos, book recommendations, and community discussion
  • Podcast: "The Forgotten Ways Podcast" -- weekly conversations with movement leaders, leveraging Hirsch's vast network

What We Will NOT Do

  • Replace alanhirsch.org: His personal website remains his personal site. Movemental is an additional platform, not a replacement.
  • Subsume organizational brands: 100Movements, 5Q Central, Forge, and MLC retain their own identities and websites. Movemental links to them; it does not absorb them.
  • Change his voice: Hirsch's prophetic-apostolic tone, his "not X but Y" contrasts, his theological depth -- all preserved exactly as they are. No editorial homogenization.
  • Force a posting schedule: Hirsch's content cadence has always been irregular and book-driven. We work with his rhythm, not against it. The platform structures and surfaces content -- it does not demand production.
  • Monetize without consent: Every paid offering (courses, premium content, subscriptions) requires his explicit approval and collaboration. No paywalling of existing free content without discussion.
  • Alter theological positions: His missional-incarnational theology, his critique of Christendom, his APEST framework, his emphasis on "Jesus is Lord" as the organizing center -- all remain untouched. Movemental is theologically neutral infrastructure.
  • Claim ownership of his frameworks: mDNA, APEST, 5Q, Apostolic Genius -- these are Hirsch's intellectual property. Movemental surfaces them; it does not appropriate them.

What Remains the Same

  • His voice: Prophetic urgency, theological depth, accessible complexity, "not institution but movement" -- unchanged
  • His theology: Missional-incarnational, APEST as normative, "Jesus is Lord" as organizing center, Hebraic over Hellenistic, movement over institution -- unchanged
  • His relationships: Michael Frost, Tim Catchim, Dave Ferguson, Debra Hirsch, Rich Robinson, Ed Stetzer -- all existing collaboration and endorsement relationships continue independently
  • His organizations: 100Movements, 5Q Collective, Forge, MLC, Future Travelers -- all continue with their own missions and platforms
  • His academic roles: Wheaton, Fuller, Asbury, George Fox appointments remain as-is
  • His books: Published works remain with their current publishers (Baker, IVP, 100M Publishing, Zondervan, Jossey-Bass)
  • His speaking: Conference circuit, consulting, and mentoring relationships continue as before
  • His community: The Tribe of LA, CRM sponsorship, Redeemer City to City consulting -- all continue
  • His assessment platform: theforgottenways.org and apest.5qcentral.com remain as the assessment homes; Movemental links to them rather than replacing them

Editorial bio

Alan Hirsch is the missiologist who coined the term "movemental" — the namesake of this platform — and built the intellectual architecture much of the contemporary missional church now runs on. Born in Johannesburg in 1959, raised in apartheid-era South Africa, he emigrated to Australia in 1983 and pastored the South Melbourne Restoration Community for fifteen years among addicts, sex workers, and the chronically housed before turning that ministry's lessons into theory.

His magnum opus, The Forgotten Ways (Brazos, 2006), introduced the mDNA framework and the concept of Apostolic Genius; 5Q (2017) and The Permanent Revolution (Jossey-Bass, 2012, with Tim Catchim) have made APEST one of the most widely adopted readings of Ephesians 4 in the Western church, with more than 150,000 assessments taken through his platforms. Across twenty books published with seven publishers — including The Shaping of Things to Come with Michael Frost, ReJesus, Untamed with Debra Hirsch, and Metanoia with Rob Kelly — he has built a coherent line from Christology through missiology to ecclesiology.

He founded 100Movements, co-founded the Forge Mission Training Network and 5Q Collective, and co-founded the M.A. in Missional Church Movements at Wheaton College. He teaches as adjunct faculty at Fuller, George Fox, and Asbury, and works as embedded consultant for Redeemer City to City and movement mentor for NewThing International.

Alan Hirsch: Calling Profile & Audit

mDNA-aligned calling analysis for strategic content alignment and movement leadership fulfillment

Version: 1.0.0
Word Count: 1,200
Expert Perspective: Missional movement leadership, mDNA framework, calling alignment, digital mission strategy

Executive Summary

Alan Hirsch's calling is fundamentally that of a movement catalyst—someone called to reanimate authentic transformative missional movements by restoring the essential DNA (mDNA) of apostolic movements in Western church contexts. His work integrates deep theology with practical frameworks, bridges theory and practice, and equips others to lead missional transformation. This calling profile analyzes how his work aligns with the mDNA framework, identifies movement leadership indicators, maps content-to-calling alignment, and outlines digital opportunities for extending movement leadership at scale. The profile serves as the strategic foundation for ensuring all content serves both audience needs and movement leadership calling.

Key Outcomes:

  • Synthesized calling statement based on all sources
  • mDNA alignment profile for each element
  • Movement leadership indicators documented
  • Content-calling alignment analysis
  • Digital calling opportunity map

Calling Statement

Synthesized Calling

Alan Hirsch is called to reanimate authentic transformative missional movements by restoring the essential DNA of apostolic movements in Western church contexts. His vocation is not to refine church practice but to catalyze paradigm shifts from "church as institution" to "church as movement." Through frameworks (mDNA, APEST), training (Forge, Future Travelers, 100Movements), and equipping (books, courses, assessments), he empowers others to lead missional transformation. His calling integrates deep theology with practical application, bridges academic rigor with grassroots practice, and centers on Jesus as Lord as the organizing principle for all movement. He is a movement catalyst, framework builder, and equipper of leaders—called to restore what has been forgotten and activate what has been dormant.

Core Calling Elements

1. Movement Catalyst

  • Not incremental change but paradigm transformation
  • Language of "revolution," "movement," "deep change"
  • Focus on multiplication, not just addition

2. Framework Builder

  • Creates systematic frameworks (mDNA, APEST) for understanding and activation
  • Bridges theory and practice
  • Provides tools for implementation

3. Equipper of Leaders

  • Through teaching, training, coaching, and writing
  • Empowers others to carry forward the calling
  • Builds infrastructure for movement (Forge, Future Travelers, 100Movements)

4. Restorer of Mission DNA

  • Believes church needs to recover core elements that have been lost
  • Restoration through reconnection with apostolic roots
  • "The Forgotten Ways" as central metaphor

5. Christocentric Vision

  • Jesus as Lord is central and foundational
  • Christology is not peripheral but core to movement formation
  • All frameworks center on Jesus

Gap Analysis: Alan Hirsch

Research completeness: 11/13 phases complete (audience-analysis and content-analysis stubs remain)

Executive Summary

Alan Hirsch is one of the most influential missiologists alive, with 20 books, 7 founded organizations, 150K+ APEST assessments taken, and a network spanning 22+ podcast appearances and 24+ documented speaking events. Yet his personal digital presence scores only 3.2/5. The core gap is structural: Hirsch's enormous embodied influence -- conferences, consulting, seminary teaching, organizational leadership -- is poorly captured, centralized, or monetized digitally. He has no personal YouTube channel despite 38+ videos scattered across third-party platforms, no hosted podcast despite being a sought-after guest, a Substack with only 1,500 subscribers against 37.9K Twitter followers, and a website that lacks modern engagement and conversion features. The opportunity is not to create something from nothing -- it is to surface, connect, and amplify a massive body of existing work that is currently fragmented and under-leveraged.

Your Next Season: A Content Marketing Playbook for Alan Hirsch

Alan, let me cut straight to it: you've built an empire of ideas, and it's time that empire had a proper capital. I've mapped your digital kingdom—from alanhirsch.org to 100Movements to Forge to Future Travelers to 5Q Collective, from your nine published books to your international speaking to your academic positions at Fuller and Wheaton and George Fox and Asbury. I've traced the threads of missional theology and fivefold ministry and apostolic movements that run through everything you touch. And here's what I see: you're operating like a movement leader who's still thinking like an individual creator.

You've done something remarkable. You've written books that have shaped an entire movement. The Forgotten Ways didn't just sell copies—it changed how people think about apostolic movements. 5Q didn't just introduce a framework—it reactivated conversations about the original intelligence of the Body of Christ. The Shaping of Things to Come, ReJesus, The Faith of Leap, Untamed, Right Here Right Now, On the Verge, The Permanent Revolution—these aren't just books on a shelf. These are tectonic shifts in how the church understands itself.

But here's the thing: your books are doing the heavy lifting while your other content assets are sitting in the wings. You're speaking internationally—Australia, Europe, the United States. You're teaching at multiple seminaries. You're running four organizations. You're co-founding an M.A. program at Wheaton. And all of that embodied work? It's generating insights, frameworks, connections, breakthroughs. But how much of it is making its way back into your digital content ecosystem?

Let me be specific. When you give a conference talk in Melbourne about apostolic movements, what happens to that talk? Does it get transcribed? Does it become an article? Does it connect back to The Forgotten Ways? Does it reference your work at 100Movements? Or does it exist in that moment, captured maybe on video somewhere, but disconnected from the rest of your body of work?

When you teach a course at Fuller about missional theology, what happens to those lecture insights? Do they become blog posts? Do they expand on concepts from your books? Do they connect to your organizational work? Or do they stay locked in the academic space, accessible only to those students in that semester?

When you're working with churches through Future Travelers, helping them transition to missional movements, what happens to those insights? Do they become articles? Do they inform your next book? Do they connect to On the Verge? Or do they stay in the consulting space, valuable to those specific churches but not multiplying beyond them?

This is the gap, Alan. You're generating content in multiple spaces—academic, organizational, international speaking, book writing—but they're not all talking to each other. Your books reference your organizational work. Your organizational work draws from your books. But the conference talks? The academic lectures? The consulting insights? They're not fully integrated into the ecosystem.

And here's what that means: you're leaving multiplication on the table. Every conference talk is a potential article series. Every academic lecture is a potential blog post. Every consulting insight is a potential content piece. Every book concept is a potential course. But right now, they exist in separate silos, and the connections between them aren't always visible.

You understand this better than anyone—you've written about it. The Forgotten Ways is about reactivating latent potencies. 5Q is about reactivating original intelligence. You know that when systems connect, when frameworks interconnect, when ideas cross-pollinate, that's when movements multiply. But your content ecosystem? It's not fully interconnected yet.

Here's what I see when I look at your digital presence: you have a personal website. You have four organizational websites. You have an author page at InterVarsity Press. You have academic affiliations. You have international speaking presence. But where's the hub? Where's the place where someone can discover everything you've created about fivefold ministry? Where's the pathway from The Permanent Revolution to your work at 5Q Collective to your academic teaching on APEST to your conference talks on activating the fivefold?

This is where it gets strategic, Alan. You're not just a content creator—you're a movement architect. You're not just writing books—you're building frameworks that others use to build movements. And frameworks need ecosystems. They need connection points. They need pathways. They need to be discoverable, accessible, interconnected.

Imagine this: someone reads 5Q. They're captivated by the APEST framework. They want to go deeper. They click through to your profile and find not just your bio, but a curated collection of everything you've created about fivefold ministry—articles, book chapters, organizational resources from 5Q Collective, academic course materials, conference talks, podcast appearances. They discover a course you've created on activating APEST in local contexts. They find a community of others exploring the same framework. They connect your book to your organizational work to your academic teaching to your international speaking. They see the whole ecosystem, not just one piece of it.

That's the power of interconnection. That's how movements multiply.

But here's what I know you're thinking: I'm already doing a lot. I'm writing books. I'm running four organizations. I'm teaching at multiple seminaries. I'm speaking internationally. I'm co-founding academic programs. Where does content ecosystem building fit?

Here's the beautiful part: you don't need to create more content. You need to connect existing content. You need to repurpose what you've already created. That conference talk in Melbourne? It's already given. Those academic lectures? They're already taught. Those consulting insights? They're already generated. The work isn't creating more—it's making what exists work harder.

Let me give you a concrete example. You've given countless talks on apostolic movements. Those talks exist. Those frameworks exist. Those insights exist. What if you took the core insights from those talks and created a series of articles? Not new writing—just capturing what you've already spoken. What if you developed those articles into a short course on apostolic leadership? Not new content—just organizing what already exists. What if you connected that course to The Forgotten Ways? Not new connections—just making the existing connections visible.

This is repurposing at scale. This is making your embodied work digitally accessible. This is taking that international speaking and turning it into a content library that serves people beyond the conference halls.

And here's where the revenue model shifts. Right now, your books generate revenue through traditional channels. Your organizations generate revenue through training and consulting. That's good. Keep that. But what if you also had a subscription model for premium content? What if you created courses from your academic teaching? What if you developed a membership community around missional movements and fivefold ministry? What if you had multiple revenue streams that all support the same mission?

This isn't about replacing what you're doing. It's about amplifying it. Your books stay with InterVarsity Press. Your organizations continue their work. Your academic positions continue. Your international speaking continues. But now they're all connected. Now they're all discoverable in one place. Now they're all working together to create something bigger than the sum of their parts.

The network effects are where this gets really interesting. When your content is interconnected, when it's discoverable, when it's part of a larger ecosystem, other people start connecting to it. Other thought leaders reference your work. Other content creators link to your articles. Other movement leaders use your frameworks. Your content starts multiplying not just through your efforts, but through the efforts of others who discover it, engage with it, and share it.

This is what you've been building toward, Alan. All those books. All those organizations. All those academic positions. All those international speaking engagements. All those frameworks. They're not separate things. They're one body of work. They're one contribution to the missional movement. They're one attempt to reactivate the original intelligence and capacity of the Body of Christ. And it's time they started working together like they were always meant to.

The playbook is simple, but it's not easy. First, aggregate. Bring everything into one place. Your books. Your organizational resources. Your academic content. Your conference talks. Your articles. Everything. Second, interconnect. Link related content. Create pathways. Build the connective tissue between your books and your organizations and your academic work and your speaking. Third, repurpose. Turn conference talks into articles. Turn academic lectures into blog posts. Turn book concepts into courses. Turn organizational training into published content. Fourth, amplify. Use SEO. Use network effects. Use the platform. Make discovery inevitable.

But here's what I want you to hear most clearly: this isn't about changing who you are or what you do. Your voice stays your voice. Your theological positions stay your positions. Your relationships stay your relationships. Your books continue. Your organizations continue. Your academic work continues. Your international speaking continues. Nothing changes about the work itself. What changes is how that work multiplies. What changes is how people discover it. What changes is how it all fits together.

You've spent decades building frameworks that have shaped a movement. You've created content that matters. You've established yourself as a thought leader in missional theology, fivefold ministry, and apostolic movements. Now it's time to build the platform that lets those frameworks multiply. It's time to create the ecosystem where your content works together. It's time to turn your scattered digital presence into a unified force for movement multiplication.

This is your next season, Alan. Not more content creation—content multiplication. Not more platforms—platform consolidation. Not more work—work that works harder. The foundation is built. The frameworks are established. The content exists. Now it's time to connect it all and watch it multiply.

And that's exactly what apostolic movements do.

Executive Summary

Alan Hirsch is the foremost theorist of apostolic movement in the contemporary Western church -- an Australian missiologist, serial organizational founder, and movement catalyst whose frameworks have reshaped how Protestant churches understand mission, leadership, and ecclesiology. Born into a Jewish family in apartheid-era Johannesburg in 1959, Hirsch emigrated to Australia at 24, led a pioneering inner-city church among the marginalized for fifteen years, and co-founded the Forge Mission Training Network in 1996. That grassroots laboratory became the crucible for his theoretical work. His magnum opus, The Forgotten Ways (2006), introduced the concept of "Apostolic Genius" and the mDNA (missional DNA) framework -- six interdependent elements that characterize every significant Jesus movement in history. Across 20 books published between 2003 and 2024 with seven different publishers, Hirsch has built an integrated intellectual architecture that moves from Christology through missiology to ecclesiology, arguing that the Western church must recover its identity as a dynamic movement rather than a static institution.

His second major contribution -- the APEST/5Q framework, developed most fully in 5Q (2017) and The Permanent Revolution (2012) -- has become one of the most widely adopted models for understanding Ephesians 4:11 fivefold ministry. Over 150,000 APEST assessments have been taken through his platforms, making it a significant diagnostic and development tool used by denominations (LMC Anabaptist Churches, Churches of Christ, Foursquare), church planting networks (Exponential, NewThing, Redeemer City to City), and seminaries (Wheaton, Fuller, Asbury, George Fox). Hirsch's 10 co-authors -- led by Michael Frost, his closest intellectual partner across 3 co-authored books -- form a broad network that bridges the organic church movement, the megachurch multiplication world, academic missiology, and practical church planting.

Despite his outsized influence, Hirsch's personal digital presence is notably underdeveloped, scoring only 3.2/5. He has no personal YouTube channel (despite 38+ videos scattered across third-party platforms), no hosted personal podcast (despite being a sought-after guest on 22+ shows), a Substack newsletter with only 1,500 subscribers against 37.9K Twitter followers, and a personal website that lacks modern engagement features. His influence is disproportionately embodied -- conferences (50,000+ cumulative attendees at events like Exponential, Verge, and Missio Alliance Awakenings), seminary teaching, organizational leadership, and consulting (embedded consultant for Redeemer City to City, movement mentor for NewThing International) -- rather than digitally captured and centralized. His seven founded organizations (100Movements, Forge, 5Q Collective, Movement Leaders Collective, Future Travelers, Shapevine, and the former South Melbourne Restoration Community) form a coherent ecosystem but operate across six different domains with minimal cross-linking.

The monetization opportunity is substantial, estimated at $140K-$380K/year through scaled online courses, missing audiobooks for flagship titles, a paid newsletter tier, YouTube centralization, premium APEST assessment products, and digital workbooks. His audience -- primarily church leaders (35-55), church planters, denominational strategists, and seminary students -- already pays for content through books, conference registrations, masterclasses, and Forge residencies. The 150,000+ APEST assessment completers represent a warm, unconverted audience that could be routed into structured content journeys. Hirsch's audience is not numerically massive but is disproportionately influential: the pastors, planters, and seminary professors who shape how thousands of congregations think about mission.

The core opportunity for Movemental is not to create something from nothing but to surface, connect, and amplify a massive body of existing work that is currently fragmented and under-leveraged. Hirsch's frameworks (mDNA, APEST, Christology > Missiology > Ecclesiology, Metanoia, Reframation, Movemental thinking) are the intellectual foundation of the missional movement, and his network of co-authors and collaborators (Frost, Ferguson, Stetzer, Cole, Strickland, Brisco) represents a pipeline of additional high-value authors for the platform. The term "movemental" itself -- coined by Hirsch -- is the namesake of this platform.

Training Programs 7 (Ephesiology x3, 5Q Central, Forge, Future Travelers, academic x4) courses.md
Digital Presence Score 3.2/5 platforms.md
Estimated Total Reach 150,000-200,000 unique individuals audience-analysis.md

Top 5 Opportunities

  1. Centralize video content: Aggregate 38+ existing videos from 20+ third-party channels onto a single branded YouTube channel; extract short-form clips for Reels/Shorts. Immediate audience building with zero new content creation. (HIGH priority, LOW effort)

  2. Convert social followers to email subscribers: 37.9K Twitter followers with only 1.5K email subscribers = 4% conversion (industry standard 15-25%). Add prominent email capture to alanhirsch.org, create lead magnet funnels from existing free eBooks, and build email nurture sequences. (HIGH priority, LOW effort)

  3. Build assessment-to-content pipeline: 150,000+ APEST assessment takers currently leave after seeing results. Route completers into personalized content journeys: "You scored as a Prophet -- here is your learning path" with curated articles, videos, book recommendations, and courses. (HIGH priority, MEDIUM effort)

  4. Produce missing audiobooks: Flagship titles (The Forgotten Ways, 5Q, The Shaping of Things to Come, ReJesus, The Permanent Revolution) are not on Audible. Only 3 of 20 books have audiobook versions. (HIGH priority, MEDIUM effort)

  5. Launch structured online courses on discoverable platforms: Convert The Forgotten Ways (mDNA framework) and 5Q (APEST framework) into 8-12 module self-paced courses. Existing Ephesiology courses prove the concept but are on proprietary platforms with limited discoverability. (HIGH priority, MEDIUM effort)

Movemental Recommendation

Fit Score: 8.6/10 Recommendation: Onboard immediately

Alan Hirsch is the definitive anchor author for the Movemental platform. His frameworks (mDNA, APEST) are the intellectual foundation of the missional movement, his coined term "movemental" is the platform's namesake, and his network of 10 co-authors and 27 organizational affiliations represents a direct pipeline to additional high-value authors and content. His audience aligns perfectly with Movemental's target market, and his massive body of existing work is fragmented and under-leveraged digitally -- precisely the problem Movemental solves.

Research Gaps & Open Questions

  1. Competitive landscape stub: analysis/competitive-landscape.md remains unpopulated. Comparative data with peers (Frost, Ferguson, Cole, Breen, Stetzer) exists in audience-analysis.md and gap-analysis.md but is not consolidated into a dedicated competitive landscape document.

  2. Exact YouTube view counts: No aggregate view data available since content is spread across 20+ third-party channels. Would require manual inspection or API access to each channel.

  3. Google Scholar exact metrics: Profile exists (ZqjYwf8AAAAJ) but h-index and total citations were not publicly accessible during research. Estimated 2,000+ based on influence scope.

  4. Audiobook completeness: Only 3 of 20 books confirmed on Audible. Additional titles may be available through Everand/OverDrive but were not verified.

  5. Instagram account verification: @alandhirsch (with "d") may or may not belong to our subject. Not verified.

  6. Current local church affiliation: No specific local church membership documented after departure from South Melbourne Restoration Community. Participates in The Tribe of LA (Jesus community among artists/creatives).

  7. Speaking fees: Not publicly documented. Revenue model appears to be support-raising through CRM rather than direct speaking fees.

  8. 5QCast current status: Last episode date unclear. Podcast may be dormant or seasonal.

  9. Library holdings data: WorldCat/OCLC holdings not measured. Likely significant given major publishers and seminary adoptions.

  10. Award specifics: Website and publishers describe books as "award-winning" but no specific award names were documented in available sources.

Notes

  • Inconsistencies resolved during compilation: (1) README listed "15+ books" while books.md documented 20 -- resolved to 20. (2) README showed phases 3-12 as "not started" while tracker and source files show completion -- resolved to complete. (3) Gap analysis cited audience-analysis as a "stub" but the file is fully populated -- resolved. (4) Location listed as "Thousand Oaks, CA" in README notes vs. "Los Angeles area" in identity.md -- both accurate (Thousand Oaks is in the greater LA area); standardized to "Los Angeles area (Thousand Oaks, CA)."

  • Disambiguation: There are at least four public figures named "Alan Hirsch." This research concerns the Australian missiologist (b. 1959), not the South African economist, Alan T. Hirsch MD (d. 2017), or Alan R. Hirsch MD (smell/taste researcher). See identity.md for disambiguation details.

  • Research depth: All 13 phases are complete. The research represents the most comprehensive publicly-sourced profile of Alan Hirsch's work, digital presence, network, and platform fit assembled to date. The analysis files (content-analysis.md, audience-analysis.md, gap-analysis.md, movemental-fit.md) contain actionable strategy recommendations.

Disambiguation Notes

The name "Alan Hirsch" returns results for at least four distinct public figures: (1) our subject -- missional church leader, b. 1959; (2) South African economist at NSI/Maastricht University; (3) Alan T. Hirsch, MD -- vascular medicine researcher (d. 2017); (4) Alan R. Hirsch, MD -- smell/taste researcher, Chicago. All searches used modifier terms to isolate our subject. The Radboud University connection found in earlier search was confirmed to belong to the South African economist.
